Table 5: Logit estimates for the co-jump probability.

| Variable | (1) | (2) | (3) | (4) | (5) |
| --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
| Panel A: Firm-level |
| Intercept | $\underset{(0.015)}{-2.960}$ | $\underset{(0.017)}{-3.020}$ | $\underset{(0.043)}{-3.595}$ | $\underset{(0.019)}{-2.900}$ | $\underset{(0.046)}{-3.611}$ |
| $IP$ | $\underset{(0.048)}{0.354}$ | $\underset{(0.049)}{0.368}$ |
| log( $V^{\text{NAF}}$ ) | $\underset{(0.009)}{0.132}$ | $\underset{(0.009)}{0.148}$ |
| log( $V^{\text{AF}}$ ) | $\underset{(0.010)}{0.063}$ | $\underset{(0.010)}{0.046}$ |
| $D^{\text{EARLY}}$ | $\underset{(0.036)}{0.060}$ | $\underset{(0.036)}{0.117}$ |
| $D^{\text{LATE}}$ | $\underset{(0.041)}{-0.372}$ | $\underset{(0.041)}{-0.417}$ |
| Panel B: Market-index |
| Intercept | $\underset{(0.137)}{-2.910}$ | $\underset{(0.153)}{-3.063}$ | $\underset{(0.363)}{-4.036}$ | $\underset{(0.364)}{-3.941}$ |
| $D^{\#\text{AF}}$ | $\underset{(0.360)}{1.287}$ | $\underset{(0.381)}{0.958}$ |
| log( $CV^{\text{AF}}$ ) | $\underset{(0.098)}{0.359}$ | $\underset{(0.103)}{0.293}$ |

Note. In Panel A, we estimate the logit regression: $P\big(J_{jt}^{\text{NAF}}=1\mid EA_{it}=1\big)=F\big(a+b_{1}IP_{ij}+b_{2}\log(V_{jt}^{\text{NAF}})+b_{3}\log(V_{it}^{\text{AF}})+b_{4}D^{\text{EARLY}}_{it}+b_{5}D^{\text{LATE}}_{it}\big)$ , where $F$ is the logistic distribution function. $J_{jt}^{\text{NAF}}$ is equal to one if there is a jump in the price of the non-announcing firm (NAF) $j$ on day $t$ , zero otherwise. $EA_{it}$ is equal to one if the announcing firm (AF) $i$ releases its quarterly earnings report on day $t$ , zero otherwise. $IP_{ij}$ is an industry proximity score between 0 (no overlap) and 1 (perfect overlap) calculated from the SIC codes of company $i$ and $j$ following Wang and Zajac ([2007](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#bib.bib105)). $V^{\text{NAF}}$ and $V^{\text{AF}}$ are the average number of transactions in the NAF and AF in the after-hours market in the month prior to $t$ . $D^{\text{EARLY}}_{it}$ and $D^{\text{LATE}}_{it}$ are indicator variables of whether the AF discloses its fiscal statement early or late in the earnings cycle. In Panel B, we estimate the logit regression: $P(J_{t}^{\text{SPY}}=1\mid\#\text{AF}_{t}\geq 1)=F\left(a+b_{1}D_{t}^{\#\text{AF}}+b_{2}\log(CV_{t}^{\text{AF}})\right)$ . Here, $J_{t}^{\text{SPY}}$ is equal to one if there is a jump in the market index (proxied by SPY) on day $t$ , zero otherwise. Moreover, $D_{t}^{\#\text{AF}}$ is equal to one if $\#\text{AF}_{t}\geq 5$ , zero otherwise, where $\#\text{AF}_{t}=\sum_{i}EA_{it}$ is the number of AFs on day $t$ . $CV_{t}^{\text{AF}}=\sum_{i\,:\,EA_{it}=1}V_{it}^{\text{AF}}$ is the cumulative average number of transactions in the AFs in the after-hours market in the month prior to $t$ . The table reports parameter estimates of the full model in column (5) and of restricted versions in column (1)–(4). Standard errors are shown in parenthesis below the parameter estimate. The number of observations is 97,811 in Panel A and 1,084 in Panel B.

Table 6: Post-announcement return regression.

| Variable | (1) | (2) | (3) | (4) |
| --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
| Intercept | $\underset{(0.075)}{0.145}$ | $\underset{(0.108)}{-0.370}$ | $\underset{(0.132)}{-0.745}$ | $\underset{(0.131)}{-0.731}$ |
| $z_{\text{EPS}}^{+}$ | $\underset{(0.037)}{0.345}$ | $\underset{(0.037)}{0.281}$ | $\underset{(0.037)}{0.267}$ |
| $z_{\text{EPS}}^{-}$ | $\underset{(0.157)}{0.784}$ | $\underset{(0.155)}{0.327}$ | $\underset{(0.149)}{0.330}$ |
| $\sqrt{RV_{n}^{*}}$ | $\underset{(0.074)}{0.165}$ | $\underset{(0.074)}{0.159}$ |
| $N_{A}$ | $\underset{(0.006)}{0.014}$ | $\underset{(0.006)}{0.013}$ |
| $OI$ | $\underset{(0.188)}{1.394}$ |
| $\bar{R}^{2}$ | 0.0682 | 0.0795 | 0.0960 |
| $P$ -value | 0.0016 | 0.7682 | 0.6811 |

Note. We estimate the linear regression: $r_{1m,it}^{\text{EA}}=a+b_{1}z_{\text{EPS},it}^{+}+b_{2}z_{\text{EPS},it}^{-}+b_{3}\sqrt{RV_{n,it}^{*}}D_{it}+b_{4}N_{A,it}D_{it}+b_{5}OI_{it}+\epsilon_{it}$ . $r_{1m,it}^{\text{EA}}$ is the one-minute post-announcement return, $z_{\text{EPS},it}^{+}$ ( $z_{\text{EPS},it}^{-}$ ) is the standardized unexpected earnings for positive (negative) announcements, $\sqrt{RV_{n,it}^{*}}$ is the pre-averaged realized volatility calculated over the regular trading session on the announcement day, $N_{A,it}$ is the number of analyst earnings forecasts for the announcement, $OI_{it}$ is the one-minute post-announcement cumulative net order imbalance, and $D_{it}=\text{sign}(z_{\text{EPS},it})$ is the sign function. The table reports parameter estimates of the full model in column (4) and of restricted versions in column (1)–(3). Standard errors are shown in parenthesis below the parameter estimate. The number of observations is 2,193. $\bar{R}^{2}$ is the adjusted coefficient of multiple determination. The $P$ -value is for testing the hypothesis $H_{0}:b_{1}=b_{2}$ against $H_{1}:b_{1}\neq b_{2}$ .

Note. The table shows the average return in percent from a trading strategy that employs the standardized earnings surprise to predict the one-minute post-announcement return: $r_{1m,it}^{\text{EA}}=a+b_{1}z_{\text{EPS},it}^{+}+b_{2}z_{\text{EPS},it}^{-}+\epsilon_{it}$ . A long (short) position in the stock is opened if the predicted excess return is greater (smaller) than 0.5% (-0.5%). In the baseline termination rule in Panel A, the position is held until 6:30pm (EOD). In Panel B, alternative termination rules are inspected. In Panels B.1–B.5, the position is closed after a fixed number of minutes, whereas in Panels B.6–B.10 the position is closed after a fixed number of tick updates. “Trade” employs the transaction price, “Midquote” the midquote, and “BBO” the best bid and offer. “+Xs” enforces a latency delay of X seconds before entrance. The test statistic for testing that the average return is zero, based on robust standard errors, is reported in parenthesis.

## Web appendices

## Appendix A Proof of theoretical results

In this appendix, we start with a mathematical description of the dynamic of the log-price process under investigation, $p=(p_{t})_{t\geq 0}$ , which we suppose evolves over the unit time interval, $t\in[0,1]$ . As usual, randomness is described by a filtered probability space $(\Omega,\mathcal{F},(\mathcal{F}_{t})_{t\geq 0},\mathbb{P})$ . The construction of this space in a noisy high-frequency setting is outlined in Jacod and Protter ([2012](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#bib.bib70)). We decompose the cumulative intraday log-return at time $t$ , or $r_{t}$ , into a continuous part and a jump, or discontinuous, part: $r_{t}=r_{t}^{c}+r_{t}^{d}$ .

Consistent with no-arbitrage, $r^{c}=(r_{t}^{c})_{t\geq 0}$ comprises a drift and a diffusion term:

| $$r_{t}^{c}=\underbrace{\int_{0}^{t}a_{s}\mathrm{d}s}_{\text{drift}}+\underbrace{\int_{0}^{t}\sigma_{s}\mathrm{d}W_{s}}_{\text{volatility}},$$ | (38) |
| --- | --- |

where the drift $a=(a_{t})_{t\geq 0}$ is a predictable and locally bounded process, the volatility $\sigma=(\sigma_{t})_{t\geq 0}$ is an adapted and càdlàg process, and $W=(W_{t})_{t\geq 0}$ is a standard Brownian motion.

We decompose $r^{d}=(r_{t}^{d})_{t\geq 0}$ into small and big jumps:626262The separation into “small” and “large” jumps is not crucial in the analysis.

| $$r_{t}^{d}=\underbrace{\int_{0}^{t}\int_{\mathbb{R}}\delta(s,x)1_{\{|\delta(s,x)|\leq 1\}}(\mu-\nu)(\text{d}s,\text{d}x)}_{\text{``small'' jumps}}+\underbrace{\int_{0}^{t}\int_{\mathbb{R}}\delta(s,x)1_{\{|\delta(s,x)|>1\}}\mu(\text{d}s,\text{d}x)}_{\text{``big'' jumps}},$$ | (39) |
| --- | --- |

where $\mu$ is a Poisson random measure on $\mathbb{R}_{+}\times\mathbb{R}$ with compensator $\nu(\text{d}s,\text{d}x)=\text{d}sF(\text{d}x)$ . Here $F$ is a $\sigma$ -finite measure, $\delta$ is a predictable function, and $(\tau_{k})_{k\geq 1}$ is a sequence of $\mathcal{F}_{t}$ -stopping times increasing to $\infty$ such that $|\delta(\omega,s,x)|\wedge 1\leq\psi_{k}(x)$ for all $(\omega,s,x)$ with $s\leq\tau_{k}(\omega)$ and $\int_{\mathbb{R}}\psi^{\beta}_{k}(x)F(\text{d}x)<\infty$ for all $k\geq 1$ and $\beta\in[0,2]$ . $\beta$ relates to the activity of the price jumps and can be interpreted as a generalized version of the Blumenthal and Getoor ([1961](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#bib.bib28)) index for a Lévy process, see Aït-Sahalia and Jacod ([2009a](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#bib.bib3)).636363A higher value of $\beta$ increases the frequency of the small jumps. Figure [B.1](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#A2.F1) in Appendix [B](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#A2) provides an illustration of this characteristic.

Moreover, we need a condition on the volatility process and microstructure noise.

Assumption (V): $\quad\sigma$ is of the form:

| $$\sigma_{t}=\sigma_{0}+\int_{0}^{t}\tilde{a}_{s}\text{d}s+\int_{0}^{t}\tilde{\sigma}_{s}\text{d}W_{s}+\int_{0}^{t}\tilde{v}_{s}\text{d}B_{s},$$ | (40) |
| --- | --- |

where $\sigma_{0}$ is an $\mathcal{F}_{0}$ -measurable random variable, while $\tilde{a}=(\tilde{a}_{t})_{t\geq 0}$ , $\tilde{\sigma}=(\tilde{\sigma}_{t})_{t\geq 0}$ and $\tilde{v}=(\tilde{v}_{t})_{t\geq 0}$ are adapted, càdlàg processes.

Assumption (N): The microstructure noise is of the form

| $$\epsilon_{i\Delta_{n}}=\omega_{i\Delta_{n}}\pi_{i},$$ | (41) |
| --- | --- |

where (i) $\pi=(\pi_{i})_{i\geq 0}$ follows an MA( $\ell$ ) process with $\ell\in\mathbb{Z}^{+}$ , i.e. $\pi_{i}=u_{i}+\sum_{j=1}^{\ell}\theta_{j}u_{i-j}$ for some i.i.d. mean zero sequence $(u_{i})_{i\in\mathbb{Z}}$ and constants $\theta_{j}$ , $j=1,\dots,\ell$ such that $1+\sum_{j=1}^{\ell}\theta_{j}\neq 0$ . Moreover, the distribution of $\pi_{0}$ has unit variance, is symmetric, and has moments of arbitrary order. (ii) $\omega=(\omega_{t})_{t\geq 0}$ is of the form:

| $$\omega_{t}=\omega_{0}+\int_{0}^{t}\bar{a}_{s}\text{d}s+\int_{0}^{t}\bar{\sigma}_{s}\text{d}W_{s}+\int_{0}^{t}\bar{v}_{s}\text{d}B_{s},$$ | (42) |
| --- | --- |

where $\omega_{0}$ is an $\mathcal{F}_{0}$ -measurable random variable, $\bar{a}=(\bar{a}_{t})_{t\geq 0}$ , $\bar{\sigma}=(\bar{\sigma}_{t})_{t\geq 0}$ , $\bar{v}=(\bar{v}_{t})_{t\geq 0}$ are adapted, càdlàg processes, while $B=(B_{t})_{t\geq 0}$ is a standard Brownian motion and $B\perp\!\!\!\perp W$ .646464The symbol $A\perp\!\!\!\perp B$ means $A$ and $B$ are stochastically independent. (iii) $\pi\perp\!\!\!\perp(p,\omega)$ . (iv) $\limsup_{|t|\to\infty}|\chi(t)|<1$ , where $\chi$ is the characteristic function of $u_{0}$ .

Assumption (V) and Assumption (N,ii) allow us to establish the order of various approximating terms appearing in the proofs, once we freeze the volatility and noise processes locally on small blocks of high-frequency data.656565Assumption (V) can be extended to discontinuous volatility processes following Barndorff-Nielsen, Graversen, Jacod, Podolskij, and Shephard ([2006](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#bib.bib20)).

We assume without loss of generality that $a$ , $\sigma$ , $\tilde{a}$ , $\tilde{\sigma}$ , $\tilde{v},$ $\omega$ , $\bar{a}$ , $\bar{\sigma}$ , $\bar{v}$ and the jump components of $p_{t}$ are bounded. That this is innocuous follows from the localization procedure described in Jacod and Protter ([2012](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#bib.bib70), Section 4.4.1).

The conditions in Assumption (N) are weak and allow for complicated dynamics, such as heteroscedasticity, serial dependence, and endogeneity. This generalizes previous work in the pre-averaging space based on i.i.d. and exogenous noise (Christensen, Oomen, and Podolskij, [2014](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#bib.bib42); Jacod, Li, Mykland, Podolskij, and Vetter, [2009](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#bib.bib68); Podolskij and Vetter, [2009a](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#bib.bib97), [b](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#bib.bib98)). The assumption follows Jacod, Li, and Zheng ([2019](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#bib.bib69)), who assume $\pi$ is a stationary MA( $\infty$ ) process with suitable mixing condition. Here, for technical reasons, we restrict the noise to be at most $\ell$ -dependent, for a finite (but arbitrarily large) $\ell$ .

The theory also imposes weak regularity conditions on the weight function $g$ , namely $g:[0,1]\rightarrow\mathbb{R}$ is continuous and piecewise continuously differentiable with piecewise Lipshitz derivative $g^{\prime}$ , $g(0)=g(1)=0$ , and $\int_{0}^{1}g(s)^{2}\mathrm{d}s>0$ .

Next, we prove our mathematical results. We employ a generic constant $C$ to bound various terms. Its value may change from line to line without notice.

### A.1 An extended central limit theorem

We start by presenting an extended version of Theorem [1](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#Thmtheorem1) for a generalized pre-averaged bipower variation estimator. With a slight abuse of the notation from the main text, we define here the estimator with an alternative normalization. We further omit the bias-correction part. This delivers an estimator that is more convenient to work with in the derivations:

| $$BV_{n}(q,r)=\frac{1}{n}\sum_{i=0}^{n-2k_{n}+1}|n^{1/4}\bar{r}_{i}^{*}|^{q}|n^{1/4}\bar{r}_{i+k_{n}}^{*}|^{r},$$ | (43) |
| --- | --- |

where $q,r\in\mathcal{S}\equiv\{0\}\cup[1,\infty)$ .

We introduce the following notation:

| $$\psi_{1}^{n}=k_{n}\sum_{j=0}^{k_{n}-1}(g_{j+1}^{n}-g_{j}^{n})^{2}\quad\text{and}\quad\psi_{2}^{n}=\frac{1}{k_{n}}\sum_{j=1}^{k_{n}}(g_{j}^{n})^{2}.$$ |
| --- |

Furthermore,

| $$c_{1}^{n}=\frac{1}{k_{n}\psi_{2}^{n}}\quad\text{and}\quad c_{2}^{n}=\frac{\psi_{1}^{n}}{\psi_{2}^{n}\theta^{2}}.$$ |
| --- |

After Podolskij and Vetter ([2009a](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#bib.bib97)) and Hautsch and Podolskij ([2013](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#bib.bib64)), we expect that under $\mathcal{H}_{0}$

| $$BV_{n}(q,r)\overset{p}{\longrightarrow}V(q,r)=\mu_{q}\mu_{r}\int_{0}^{1}\Big(\theta\psi_{2}\sigma_{s}^{2}+\frac{1}{\theta}\psi_{1}\rho^{2}\omega^{2}_{s}\Big)^{\frac{q+r}{2}}\text{d}s,$$ |
| --- |

where $\mu_{s}=\mathbb{E}\big[|N(0,1)|^{s}\big]$ and $\rho^{2}$ is the long-run variance of the noise process $\pi$ , which is given by $\rho^{2}=\rho(0)+2\sum_{m=1}^{\ell}\rho(m)$ , where $\rho(m)=\mathbb{E}[\pi_{0}\pi_{m}]$ is the $m$ th autocovariance. Moreover,

| $$\psi_{1}^{n}\rightarrow\psi_{1}=\int_{0}^{1}[g^{\prime}(s)]^{2}\text{d}s\quad\text{and}\quad\psi_{2}^{n}\rightarrow\psi_{2}=\int_{0}^{1}g(s)^{2}\text{d}s.$$ |
| --- |

###### Theorem 3.

Assume that $r$ follows the process in ([3](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#S2.E3)) with $r_{t}^{d}\equiv 0$ (for all $t$ ), and that Assumptions (V) and (N) hold. As $n\rightarrow 0$ , for $q_{1},q_{2},r_{1},r_{2}\in\mathcal{S}$ ,

| $$n^{1/4}\begin{pmatrix}BV_{n}(q_{1},r_{1})-V(q_{1},r_{1})\\[2.84544pt] BV_{n}(q_{2},r_{2})-V(q_{2},r_{2})\end{pmatrix}\overset{\mathcal{D}_{s}}{\longrightarrow}\text{MN}(0,\tilde{\Sigma}),$$ |
| --- |

where $\tilde{\Sigma}$ is defined in ([45](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#A1.E45)) and $\overset{\mathcal{D}_{s}}{\longrightarrow}$ denotes stable convergence in law.

To present an expression for the asymptotic covariance matrix in the above central limit theorem, we need to introduce some additional notation. In particular, for $i,j\in\{1,2\}$ , we set

| $$h_{ij}(x,y,z)=\mathrm{cov}(|H_{1}|^{q_{i}}|H_{2}|^{r_{i}},|H_{3}|^{q_{j}}|H_{4}|^{r_{j}}),$$ | (44) |
| --- | --- |

where $x,y$ are two-dimensional vectors, whereas $z$ is a four-dimensional vector, and $(H_{1},H_{2},H_{3},H_{4})$ are centered multivariate normal distributed random variables with covariance structure:

(i)

$\mathbb{E}\big[|H_{i}|^{2}]=y_{1}x_{1}^{2}+y_{2}x_{2}^{2},\quad i\in\{1,2,3,4\}$ ,

(ii)

$H_{1}\perp H_{2},\quad H_{1}\perp H_{4},\quad H_{3}\perp H_{4}$ ,

(iii)

$\mathrm{cov}(H_{1},H_{3})=\mathrm{cov}(H_{2},H_{4})=z_{1}x_{1}^{2}+z_{2}x_{2}^{2}$ ,

(iv)

$\mathrm{cov}(H_{2},H_{3})=z_{3}x_{1}^{2}+z_{4}x_{2}^{2}$ .

We also introduce the following functions:

| $$f_{1}(s)=\frac{1}{\theta}\phi_{1}(s),\quad f_{2}(s)=\theta\phi_{2}(s),\quad f_{3}(s)=\frac{1}{\theta}\phi_{3}(s),\quad f_{4}(s)=\theta\phi_{4}(s),$$ |
| --- |

for $s\in[0,2]$ , where

| $\displaystyle\phi_{1}(s)$ | $\displaystyle=\int_{0}^{1-s}g^{\prime}(u)g^{\prime}(u+s)\mathrm{d}s,\quad\phi_{2}(s)=\int_{0}^{1-s}g(u)g(u+s)\mathrm{d}s,$ |
| --- | --- |
| $\displaystyle\phi_{3}(2-s)$ | $\displaystyle=\int_{0}^{1-s}g^{\prime}(u)g^{\prime}(u+s-1)\mathrm{d}s,\quad\phi_{4}(s)=\int_{0}^{2-s}g(u)g(u+s-1)\mathrm{d}s.$ |

We compactly write these in vectorized form as $f(s)=(f_{1}(s),\dots,f_{4}(s))$ . The $(i,j)$ -th component of $\tilde{\Sigma}$ is then given by

| $$\tilde{\Sigma}_{ij}=2\theta\int_{0}^{1}\int_{0}^{2}h_{ij}\big((\rho\omega_{u},\sigma_{u}),(\psi_{1}/\theta,\theta\psi_{2}),f(s)\big)\mathrm{d}s\mathrm{d}u.$$ | (45) |
| --- | --- |

Compared to the estimators introduced in this appendix, the pre-averaged realized variance and bipower variation in ([11](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#S2.E11)) are merely rescaled and bias-corrected versions of ([43](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#A1.E43)) with $(q,r)=(2,0)$ and $(q,r)=(1,1)$ , since (see also Lemma [11](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#Thmtheorem11))

| $$RV_{n}^{*}=c_{1}BV_{n}(2,0)+o_{p}\big(n^{-1/4}\big)\quad\mathrm{and}\quad BV_{n}^{*}=c_{1}\frac{\pi}{2}BV_{n}(1,1)+o_{p}\big(n^{-1/4}\big),$$ |
| --- |

where

| $$c_{1}=\frac{1}{\theta\psi_{2}}.$$ |
| --- |

Hence, the relation between $\tilde{\Sigma}$ and $\Sigma$ is as follows:

| $$\Sigma_{ij}=c_{i}^{2}\left(\frac{\pi}{2}\right)^{i+j-2}\tilde{\Sigma}_{ij}.$$ | (46) |
| --- | --- |

### Proof of Theorem 3

For any $m\leq i$ , we define

| $$\bar{r}_{i,m}^{*}=\sum_{j=1}^{k_{n}}g(j/k_{n})\big(\sigma_{m\Delta_{n}}(W_{(i+j)\Delta_{n}}-W_{(i+j-1)\Delta_{n}})+\omega_{m\Delta_{n}}(\pi_{i+j}-\pi_{i+j-1})\big).$$ | (47) |
| --- | --- |

We note that $\bar{r}_{i,m}^{*}$ serves to approximate $\bar{r}_{i}^{*}$ by freezing the processes $\sigma$ and $\omega$ locally at the time point $m\Delta_{n}$ .

We also denote

| $\displaystyle\eta(q,r)_{i}^{n}$ | $\displaystyle=|n^{1/4}\bar{r}_{i}^{*}|^{q}|n^{1/4}\bar{r}_{i+k_{n}}^{*}|^{r},$ |
| --- | --- |
| $\displaystyle\eta(q,r)_{i,m}^{n}$ | $\displaystyle=|n^{1/4}\bar{r}_{i,m}^{*}|^{q}|n^{1/4}\bar{r}_{i+k_{n},m}^{*}|^{r}.$ |

Set $\mathcal{G}_{i}=\sigma(u_{j}:j\leq i)$ to be the $\sigma$ -field generated by the i.i.d. sequence $(u_{i})$ used to build the noise process. We then define the product $\sigma$ -field $\mathcal{H}_{i}^{n}=\mathcal{F}_{i\Delta_{n}}\otimes\mathcal{G}_{i-2k_{n}}$ , where $n$ is chosen large enough such that $\ell\leq 2k_{n}$ , so that the lagged form $\mathcal{G}_{i-2k_{n}}$ deals with the $\ell$ -dependence in $\pi$ .

###### Lemma 4.

Under the maintained assumptions of Theorem [3](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#Thmtheorem3), for any $q,r\in\mathcal{S}$ , and real number $v\geq 1$ , it holds that

| $$\max\Big(\mathbb{E}\big[|n^{1/4}\bar{r}_{i}^{*}|^{v}\mid\mathcal{H}_{m}^{n}\big],\mathbb{E}\big[|n^{1/4}\bar{r}_{i,m}^{*}|^{v}\mid\mathcal{H}_{m}^{n}\big]\Big)\leq C,$$ |
| --- |

and

| $$\max\Big(\mathbb{E}\big[|\eta(q,r)_{i}^{n}|^{v}\mid\mathcal{H}_{m}^{n}\big],\mathbb{E}\big[|\eta(q,r)_{i,m}^{n}|^{v}\mid\mathcal{H}_{m}^{n}\big]\Big)\leq C,$$ |
| --- |

uniformly in $i$ and $m$ .

The first bound is a standard result for the pre-averaged returns, which follows from the boundedness of $g$ , $\sigma$ and $\omega$ . Then, the second bound is due to the Cauchy-Schwarz inequality. $\blacksquare$

###### Lemma 5.

Under the maintained assumptions of Theorem [3](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#Thmtheorem3), for any $q,r\in\mathcal{S},$ it holds that

| $$\mathbb{E}\big[\eta(q,r)_{i,m}^{n}\mid\mathcal{H}_{m}^{n}\big]=\mu_{q}\mu_{r}\Big(\theta\psi_{2}\sigma_{m\Delta_{n}}^{2}+\frac{1}{\theta}\psi_{1}\rho^{2}\omega^{2}_{m\Delta_{n}}\Big)^{\frac{q+r}{2}}+o_{p}(n^{-1/4}),$$ |
| --- |

uniformly in $i$ and $m$ .

We adapt the proof of Lemma 4 in Podolskij and Vetter ([2009a](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#bib.bib97)) to $\ell$ -dependent data by applying Theorem 6.3 from Lahiri ([2003](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#bib.bib81)). Hence, the job is to verify conditions (C1)–(C6) therein, which are essentially those introduced adopted in Götze and Hipp ([1983](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#bib.bib56)). We first prove the result with $r=0$ , i.e the pre-averaged power variation estimator, which is then extended to the bipower case with $r>0$ .

We recall that

| $\displaystyle n^{1/4}\bar{r}_{i,m}^{*}$ | $\displaystyle=n^{1/4}\sum_{j=0}^{k_{n}-1}\left(\sigma_{m\Delta_{n}}g\left(\frac{j}{k_{n}}\right)(W_{(i+j)\Delta_{n}}-W_{(i+j-1)\Delta_{n}})+\omega_{m\Delta_{n}}\left(g\left(\frac{j}{k_{n}}\right)-g\left(\frac{j+1}{k_{n}}\right)\right)\pi_{i+j}\right)$ |
| --- | --- |
| $\displaystyle=k_{n}^{-1/2}\sum_{j=0}^{k_{n}-1}\left(\sigma_{m\Delta_{n}}g\left(\frac{j}{k_{n}}\right)\left(\frac{k_{n}}{n^{1/2}}\right)^{1/2}N_{i+j}+\omega_{m\Delta_{n}}\left(g\left(\frac{j}{k_{n}}\right)-g\left(\frac{j+1}{k_{n}}\right)\right)n^{1/4}k_{n}^{1/2}\pi_{i+j}\right).$ |

Note that the subsequent analysis is conditional on $\mathcal{H}_{m}^{n}$ , so that $\sigma_{m\Delta}$ , $\omega_{m\Delta}$ can be treated as non-random constants. As a result, the above is a scaled sum of $k_{n}$ terms that are $\ell$ -dependent, where $(N_{l})_{l\geq 1}$ is a i.i.d. sequence of standard normal variables. We set $\mathcal{D}_{i}=\sigma(N_{i})\otimes\sigma(u_{i})$ .

Since $n^{1/4}\bar{r}_{i,m}^{*}$ is mean zero, we denote

| $$\tau_{m,n}^{2}=\mathbb{E}\left[\big(n^{1/4}\bar{r}_{i,m}^{*}\big)^{2}\mid\mathcal{H}_{m}^{n}\right]$$ |
| --- |

as its conditional variance and consider the standardized version

| $$U_{m,n}=\tau_{m,n}^{-1}n^{1/4}\bar{r}_{i,m}^{*}\equiv k_{n}^{-1/2}\sum_{j=0}^{k_{n}-1}\Gamma_{i+j}^{m,n}.$$ |
| --- |

The first condition (C1) is about the existence of moments. Due to Assumption (N,i), the terms $\Gamma_{i+j}^{m,n}$ possess $v$ moments for any finite $v>0$ . Condition (C2) is verified, since $\Gamma_{i+j}^{m,n}$ has mean zero and unit variance. Condition (C3) is about the strong mixing condition and weak dependence structure of the summands, which we show by choosing the approximate variable as $\Gamma_{i+j}^{m,n}$ itself. Condition (C4) involves the definition of the strong mixing condition. This is obviously fulfilled in our setting, since $(\Gamma_{i+j}^{m,n})_{j=0}^{k_{n}-1}$ are $\ell$ -dependent. Moreover, condition (C5) is an approximate Markov-type property and follows immediately due to the finiteness of $\ell$ . This leaves condition (C6), which is a Cramer-type condition for weakly dependent data, which requires a bit more work. To this end, we note that (conditional further on $\mathcal{H}_{m}^{n}$ in each expectation):

| $$\mathbb{E}\left[\left|\mathbb{E}\left[\exp\big(\mathrm{i}t\big(\Gamma_{i+j-k}^{m,n}+\ldots+\Gamma_{i+j}^{m,n}+\ldots+\Gamma_{i+j+k}^{m,n}\big)\big)\mid\mathcal{D}_{i+s},s\neq j\right]\right|\right]=\left|\mathbb{E}\left[\exp\big(\mathrm{i}t\Gamma_{i+j}^{m,n}\big)\right]\right|,$$ |
| --- |

for each $r>\ell$ .

With the independence between $N_{i}$ and $\pi_{i}$ , this can be written as

| $$\exp\left(-\sigma_{m\Delta_{n}}^{2}g^{2}\left(\frac{j}{k_{n}}\right)\frac{k_{n}}{n^{1/2}}\frac{t^{2}}{2\tau_{n,m}^{2}}\right)\prod_{k=0}^{\ell}\left|\mathbb{E}\left[\exp\Big(\mathrm{i}t\omega_{m\Delta_{n}}\left(g\left(\frac{j}{k_{n}}\right)-\left(\frac{j+1}{k_{n}}\right)\right)n^{1/4}k_{n}^{1/2}\theta_{k}u_{i+j-k}\Big)\right]\right|$$ |
| --- |

with $\theta_{0}=1$ . Then, this condition holds due to Assumption (N,iv).

Setting $f(x)=|x|^{q}$ , the following Edgeworth expansion:

| $\displaystyle\left|\frac{1}{f(\tau_{n,m})}\mathbb{E}\left[f\big(n^{1/4}\bar{r}_{i,m}^{*}\big)\mid\mathcal{H}_{m}^{n}\right]-\int f(x)\mathrm{d}\Psi_{n}(x)\right|=o\big(k_{n}^{-1/2}\big),$ |
| --- |

holds uniformly in $i$ and $m$ , where $\Psi_{n}$ is the signed measure with the density

| $$\psi_{n}(x)=\phi(x)\left(1+\frac{1}{\sqrt{k_{n}}}\frac{x^{3}-3x}{6}\bar{\kappa}_{3}^{n}\right),$$ | (48) |
| --- | --- |

where $\bar{\kappa}_{3}^{n}$ is the third cumulant of $U_{m,n}$ .

As $f(x)=|x|^{q}$ is an even function and $\phi(x)(x^{3}-3x)$ is an odd function,

| $$\int f(x)\phi(x)(x^{3}-3x)\mathrm{d}x=0.$$ |
| --- |

In view of this result and the bound

| $$\tau_{n,m}^{2}-\left(\theta\psi_{2}\sigma_{m\Delta_{n}}^{2}+\frac{1}{\theta}\psi_{1}\rho^{2}\omega^{2}_{m\Delta_{n}}\right)=O\big(k_{n}^{-1}\big),$$ |
| --- |

we obtain the following version of the expansion:

| $$\mathbb{E}\big[\eta(q,0)_{i,m}^{n}\mid\mathcal{H}_{m}^{n}\big]=\mu_{q}\Big(\theta\psi_{2}\sigma_{m\Delta_{n}}^{2}+\frac{1}{\theta}\psi_{1}\rho^{2}\omega^{2}_{m\Delta_{n}}\Big)^{\frac{q}{2}}+o_{p}(n^{-1/4}).$$ | (49) |
| --- | --- |

Next, we look at the case $r\neq 0$ . We introduce the notation:

| $\displaystyle\hat{r}_{i,m}^{*}$ | $\displaystyle=\sum_{j=\ell}^{k_{n}-1}\left(\sigma_{m\Delta_{n}}g\left(\frac{j}{k_{n}}\right)(W_{(i+j)\Delta_{n}}-W_{(i+j-1)\Delta_{n}})+\omega_{m\Delta_{n}}\left(g\left(\frac{j}{k_{n}}\right)-g\left(\frac{j+1}{k_{n}}\right)\right)\pi_{i+j}\right),$ |
| --- | --- |
| $\displaystyle\check{r}_{i,m}^{*}$ | $\displaystyle=\sum_{j=0}^{k_{n}-1-\ell}\left(\sigma_{m\Delta_{n}}g\left(\frac{j}{k_{n}}\right)(W_{(i+j)\Delta_{n}}-W_{(i+j-1)\Delta_{n}})+\omega_{m\Delta_{n}}\left(g\left(\frac{j}{k_{n}}\right)-g\left(\frac{j+1}{k_{n}}\right)\right)\pi_{i+j}\right).$ |

To derive the statement in the lemma on $\mathbb{E}\big[\eta(q,r)_{i,m}^{n}\mid\mathcal{H}_{m}^{n}\big]$ , it suffices to show that:

| $$\mathbb{E}\left[|n^{1/4}\bar{r}_{i,m}^{*}|^{q}|n^{1/4}\hat{r}_{i+k_{n},m}^{*}|^{r}\mid\mathcal{H}_{m}^{n}\right]=\mu_{q}\mu_{r}\Big(\theta\psi_{2}\sigma_{m\Delta_{n}}^{2}+\frac{1}{\theta}\psi_{1}\rho^{2}\omega^{2}_{m\Delta_{n}}\Big)^{\frac{q+r}{2}}+o_{p}(n^{-1/4}),$$ |
| --- |

| $$\mathbb{E}\left[|n^{1/4}\check{r}_{i,m}^{*}|^{q}\big(|n^{1/4}\bar{r}_{i+k_{n},m}^{*}|^{r}-|n^{1/4}\hat{r}_{i+k_{n},m}^{*}|^{r}\big)\mid\mathcal{H}_{m}^{n}\right]=o_{p}(n^{-1/4}),$$ |
| --- |

and

| $$\mathbb{E}\left[\big||n^{1/4}\bar{r}_{i,m}^{*}|^{q}-|n^{1/4}\check{r}_{i,m}^{*}|^{q}\big|\big||n^{1/4}\bar{r}_{i+k_{n},m}^{*}|^{r}-n^{1/4}\hat{r}_{i+k_{n},m}^{*}|^{r}\big|\mid\mathcal{H}_{m}^{n}\right]=O_{p}(n^{-1/2}).$$ |
| --- |

The first and second bounds exploit that $\bar{r}_{i,m}^{*}$ and $\hat{r}_{i+k_{n},m}^{*}$ , and also $\check{r}_{i,m}^{*}$ and $(\bar{r}_{i+k_{n},m}^{*},\hat{r}_{i+k_{n},m}^{*})$ are independent, conditional on $\mathcal{H}_{m}^{n}$ , combined with an Edgeworth expansion as in ([49](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#A1.E49)) for $\check{r}_{i,m}^{*}$ and $\hat{r}_{i+k_{n},m}^{*}$ , and Lemma [4](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#Thmtheorem4). The third bound relies on the inequality $||x|^{s}-|y|^{s}|\leq C|x-y|\max(|x|^{s-1},|y|^{s-1})$ for $s\in\{q,r\}$ , together with Lemma [4](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#Thmtheorem4) and the Cauchy-Schwarz inequality. $\blacksquare$

The next preliminary result concerns the error of $\alpha_{i,m}^{n}\equiv n^{1/4}(\bar{r}_{i}^{*}-\bar{r}_{i,m}^{*})$ .

###### Lemma 6.

Under the maintained assumptions of Theorem [3](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#Thmtheorem3), for any real number $v\geq 2$ , it holds that

| $\displaystyle\mathbb{E}\big[|\alpha_{i,m}^{n}|^{v}\mid\mathcal{H}_{m}^{n}\big]$ | $\displaystyle\leq C\Big(n^{-v/4}+\big(\mathbb{E}\big[\Gamma(\sigma,m,i)^{n}+\Gamma(\omega,m,i)^{n}\mid\mathcal{H}_{m}^{n}\big]\big)^{v}\Big),$ |
| --- | --- |
| $\displaystyle\mathbb{E}\big[|\eta(q,r)_{i}^{n}-\eta(q,r)_{i,m}^{n}|^{v}\mid\mathcal{H}_{m}^{n}\big]$ | $\displaystyle\leq C\Big(n^{-v/4}+\big(\mathbb{E}\big[\Gamma(\sigma,m,i)^{n}+\Gamma(\omega,m,i)^{n}\mid\mathcal{H}_{m}^{n}\big]\big)^{v}\Big),$ |

where for any process $\gamma$ we set

| $$\Gamma(\gamma,m,i)^{n}=\sup_{m\Delta_{n}\leq s\leq(i+2k_{n})\Delta_{n}}|\gamma_{s}-\gamma_{m\Delta_{n}}|.$$ |
| --- |

By construction

| $\displaystyle\alpha_{i,m}^{n}$ | $\displaystyle=n^{1/4}\int_{i\Delta_{n}}^{(i+k_{n})\Delta_{n}}g_{n}(s-i\Delta_{n})\big[a_{s}\mathrm{d}s+(\sigma_{s}-\sigma_{m\Delta_{n}})\mathrm{d}W_{s}\big]$ |
| --- | --- |
| $\displaystyle+n^{1/4}\sum_{j=0}^{k_{n}-1}\big[g(j/k_{n})-g(j+1/k_{n})\big](\omega_{(i+j)\Delta_{n}}-\omega_{m\Delta_{n}})\pi_{i+j},$ |

where $g_{n}(s)=\sum_{j=1}^{k_{n}}g(j/k_{n})1_{\{(j-1)\Delta_{n},j\Delta_{n}\}}(s)$ . Note that $g$ , $a$ and $\sigma$ are bounded and $|g(j/k_{n})-g(j+1/k_{n})|\leq C/k_{n}$ . Furthermore, $\omega$ is bounded and independent of $\pi$ . The first result then follows from the Burkholder-Davis-Gundy inequality and $\Gamma$ being bounded. The second applies Hölder’s inequality and the inequalities $||y|^{q}-|x|^{q}|\leq C|x-y|\max(|x|^{q-1},|y|^{q-1})$ and $|y_{1}y_{2}-x_{1}x_{2}|\leq|y_{1}-x_{1}||y_{2}|+|x_{1}||y_{2}-x_{2}|$ . $\blacksquare$ We proceed by applying the “big blocks–small blocks” technique of Jacod, Li, Mykland, Podolskij, and Vetter ([2009](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#bib.bib68)). To this end, we fix an integer $p\geq 2$ , which in the later stages of the proof tends to infinity, and introduce the notation

| $\displaystyle a_{j}(p)$ | $\displaystyle=2j(p+2)k_{n},\quad b_{j}(p)=2j(p+2)k_{n}+2pk_{n},$ |
| --- | --- |
| $\displaystyle A_{j}(p)$ | $\displaystyle=\mathbb{Z}\cap[a_{j}(p),b_{j}(p)),\quad B_{j}(p)=\mathbb{Z}\cap[b_{j}(p),a_{j+1}(p)).$ |

$A_{j}(p)$ is the big block, which has size $2pk_{n}$ , while the small block $B_{j}(p)$ only has a size of $4k_{n}$ . The small blocks are going to separate the big blocks to account for the dependence introduced by pre-averaging and the MA( $\ell$ ) structure in the noise process $\pi$ . This means that some important terms (defined later) are conditionally independent, since the summands in the pre-averaged bipower estimator employ $2k_{n}$ underlying high-frequency returns. We let $j_{n}(p)\equiv\lfloor n/2(p+2)k_{n}\rfloor$ be the number of such big block–small block pairs. We utilize these pairwise blocks until observation $i_{n}(p)\equiv j_{n}(p)2(p+2)k_{n}$ , while leaving some residual unused data at the end.

Now, we define

| $$\zeta(p,q,r,1)_{j}^{n}=\sum_{u=a_{j}(p)}^{b_{j}(p)-1}\tilde{Y}_{u}^{n}(q,r)\qquad\text{and}\qquad\zeta(p,q,r,2)_{j}^{n}=\sum_{u=b_{j}(p)}^{a_{j+1}(p)-1}\tilde{Y}_{u}^{n}(q,r).$$ |
| --- |

with

| $$\tilde{Y}_{u}^{n}(q,r)=\begin{cases}n^{-1/2}\left(\eta(q,r)_{u,a_{j}(p)}^{n}-\mathbb{E}\big[\eta(q,r)_{u,a_{j}(p)}^{n}\mid\mathcal{H}_{a_{j}(p)}^{n}\big]\right),\quad u\in A_{j}(p),\\[7.11317pt] n^{-1/2}\left(\eta(q,r)_{u,b_{j}(p)}^{n}-\mathbb{E}\big[\eta(q,r)_{u,b_{j}(p)}^{n}\mid\mathcal{H}_{b_{j}(p)}^{n}\big]\right),\quad u\in B_{j}(p),\\[7.11317pt] n^{-1/2}\left(\eta(q,r)_{u,i_{n}(p)}^{n}-\mathbb{E}\big[\eta(q,r)_{u,i_{n}(p)}^{n}\mid\mathcal{H}_{i_{n}(p)}^{n}\big]\right),\quad u\in i_{n}(p),\end{cases}$$ |
| --- |

and denote

| $\displaystyle M(p,q,r)^{n}$ | $\displaystyle=n^{-1/2}\sum_{j=0}^{j_{n}(p)-1}\zeta(p,q,r,1)_{j}^{n},$ |
| --- | --- |
| $\displaystyle N(p,q,r)^{n}$ | $\displaystyle=n^{-1/2}\sum_{j=0}^{j_{n}(p)-1}\zeta(p,q,r,2)_{j}^{n},$ |
| $\displaystyle C(p,q,r)^{n}$ | $\displaystyle=n^{-1/2}\sum_{u=i_{n}(p)}^{n}\tilde{Y}_{u}^{n}(q,r).$ |

Next, we introduce the decomposition

| $$n^{1/4}(BV_{n}(q,r)-V(q,r))=n^{1/4}\left[M(p,q,r)^{n}+N(p,q,r)^{n}+C(p,q,r)^{n}\right]+F(p,q,r)^{n},$$ | (50) |
| --- | --- |

which implicitly defines $F(p,q,r)^{n}$ as a remainder term. In a series of lemmas, we will show that $M(p,q,r)^{n}$ is the leading term, while the remaining parts are asymptotically negligible.

###### Lemma 7.

Let $p\geq 2$ be fixed. Under the maintained assumptions of Theorem [3](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#Thmtheorem3), it holds that

| $$\mathbb{E}\left[\left(n^{1/4}N(p,q,r)^{n}\right)^{2}\right]\leq C/p.$$ |
| --- |

Write

| $$L_{k}^{n}=n^{-1/2}\sum_{j=0}^{k}\zeta(p,q,r,2)_{j}^{n}.$$ |
| --- |

We observe that the process $(L_{k}^{n})_{k=0}^{j_{n}(p)-1}$ is a discrete-time martingale under the filtration $(\mathcal{H}_{b_{j}(p)\Delta_{n}})_{j=0}^{j_{n}(p)-1}$ . Then, Doob’s inequality yields that

| $$\mathbb{E}\left[\left(n^{1/4}N(p,q,r)^{n}\right)^{2}\right]\leq Cn^{-1/2}\sum_{j=0}^{j_{n}(p)-1}\mathbb{E}\left[\left(\zeta(p,q,r,2)_{j}^{n}\right)^{2}\right].$$ | (51) |
| --- | --- |

Lemma [4](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#Thmtheorem4) implies that $\mathbb{E}\left[\big(\tilde{Y}_{u}^{n}(q,r)\big)^{2}\right]\leq C/n$ uniformly in $j$ , $p$ , $q$ , and $r$ . Consequently, we deduce that $\mathbb{E}\left[\left(\zeta(p,q,r,2)_{j}^{n}\right)^{2}\right]\leq C$ . Then, using $j_{n}(p)\leq Cn^{1/2}/p$ , ends the proof. $\blacksquare$

###### Lemma 8.

Let $p\geq 2$ be fixed. Under the maintained assumptions of Theorem [3](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#Thmtheorem3), it holds that

| $$\mathbb{E}\left[\left|n^{1/4}C(p,q,r)^{n}\right|\right]\leq Cpn^{-1/4}.$$ |
| --- |

Note that $n-i_{n}(p)\leq Cpn^{1/2}$ and $\mathbb{E}\left[\big|\tilde{Y}_{u}^{n}(q,r)\big|\right]\leq Cn^{-1/2}$ . This immediately leads to the conclusion that $\mathbb{E}\left[\big|n^{1/4}C(p,q,r)^{n}\big|\right]\leq Cpn^{-1/4}$ . $\blacksquare$

###### Lemma 9.

Under the maintained assumptions of Theorem [3](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#Thmtheorem3), for any $\delta>0$ , it holds that

| $$\lim_{p\rightarrow\infty}\limsup_{n\rightarrow\infty}\mathbb{P}\left[|F(p,q,r)^{n}|>\delta\right]=0.$$ |
| --- |

Recalling the decomposition in ([50](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#A1.E50)), we write that

| $$F(p,q,r)^{n}=A(p,q,r)^{n}+B(p,q,r)^{n}+D(p,q,r)^{n}-n^{1/4}C(p,q,r)^{n},$$ | (52) |
| --- | --- |

where

| $\displaystyle A(p,q,r)^{n}$ | $\displaystyle=n^{1/4}BV_{n}(q,r)-n^{1/4}\frac{1}{n}\sum_{j=0}^{j_{n}(p)-1}\sum_{u=0}^{2(p+1)k_{n}-1}\eta(q,r)_{a_{j}(p)+u,a_{j}(p)}^{n},$ |
| --- | --- |
| $\displaystyle B(p,q,r)^{n}$ | $\displaystyle=n^{1/4}\frac{1}{n}\sum_{j=0}^{j_{n}(p)-1}\sum_{u=0}^{2(p+1)k_{n}-1}\mathbb{E}\left[\eta(q,r)_{a_{j}(p)+u,a_{j}(p)}^{n}\mid\mathcal{H}_{a_{j}(p)}^{n}\right]-n^{1/4}V(q,r),$ |
| $\displaystyle D(p,q,r)^{n}$ | $\displaystyle=n^{-3/4}\sum_{j=0}^{j_{n}(p)-1}\sum_{u=0}^{2k_{n}-1}\eta(q,r)_{b_{j}(p)+u,a_{j}(p)}^{n}-\mathbb{E}\left[\eta(q,r)_{b_{j}(p)+u,a_{j}(p)}^{n}\mid\mathcal{H}_{a_{j}(p)}^{n}\right]$ |
| $\displaystyle-n^{-3/4}\sum_{j=0}^{j_{n}(p)-1}\sum_{u=0}^{2k_{n}-1}\eta(q,r)_{b_{j}(p)+u,b_{j}(p)}^{n}-\mathbb{E}\left[\eta(q,r)_{b_{j}(p)+u,b_{j}(p)}^{n}\mid\mathcal{H}_{b_{j}p)}^{n}\right]$ |

The $C(p,q,r)^{n}$ term was covered in Lemma [8](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#Thmtheorem8), while the $D(p,q,r)^{n}$ term is dealt with following Lemma [7](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#Thmtheorem7). We proceed to $A(p,q,r)^{n}$ . Ignoring asymptotically negligible remainder terms, and employing the shorthand notation $\tilde{\eta}(q,r)_{i,m}^{n}\equiv\eta(q,r)_{i}^{n}-\eta(q,r)_{i,m}^{n}$ , it is enough to study

| $\displaystyle A_{1}^{n}$ | $\displaystyle=n^{-3/4}\sum_{j=0}^{j_{n}(p)-1}\sum_{u=0}^{2(p+1)k_{n}-1}\mathbb{E}\left[\tilde{\eta}(q,r)_{a_{j}(p)+u,a_{j}(p)}^{n}\mid\mathcal{H}_{a_{j}(p)}^{n}\right],$ |
| --- | --- |
| $\displaystyle A_{2}^{n}$ | $\displaystyle=n^{-3/4}\sum_{j=0}^{j_{n}(p)-1}\sum_{u=0}^{2(p+1)k_{n}-1}\tilde{\eta}(q,r)_{a_{j}(p)+u,a_{j}(p)}^{n}-\mathbb{E}\left[\tilde{\eta}(q,r)_{a_{j}(p)+u,a_{j}(p)}^{n}\mid\mathcal{H}_{a_{j}(p)}^{n}\right].$ |

Proceeding as in the proof of Lemma [7](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#Thmtheorem7), applying Doob’s inequality and Lemma [6](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#Thmtheorem6) yields

| $\displaystyle\mathbb{E}\left[\big(A_{2}^{n}\big)^{2}\right]$ | $\displaystyle\leq Cn^{-3/2}pk_{n}\sum_{j=0}^{j_{n}(p)-1}\sum_{u=0}^{2(p+1)k_{n}-1}\mathbb{E}\left[\big(\tilde{\eta}(q,r)_{a_{j}(p)+u,a_{j}(p)}^{n}\big)^{2}\right]$ |
| --- | --- |
| $\displaystyle\leq Cn^{-3/2}pk_{n}\sum_{j=0}^{j_{n}(p)-1}\sum_{u=0}^{2(p+1)k_{n}-1}\left(n^{-1/2}+\mathbb{E}\left[\big(\Gamma(\sigma,a_{j}(p),a_{j}(p)+u)^{n}+\Gamma(\omega,a_{j}(p),a_{j}(p)+u)^{n}\big)^{2}\right]\right)$ |
| $\displaystyle\leq Cpn^{-1/2}+Cn^{-1/2}p^{2}\sum_{j=0}^{j_{n}(p)-1}\mathbb{E}\left[\big(\Gamma(\sigma,a_{j}(p),a_{j+1}(p))^{n}+\Gamma(\omega,a_{j}(p),a_{j+1}(p))^{n}\big)^{2}\right],$ |

which is negligible by Lemma 5.4 in Jacod, Li, Mykland, Podolskij, and Vetter ([2009](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#bib.bib68)).

Moving to the $A_{1}^{n}$ term we observe that, from the inequality $|y_{1}y_{2}-x_{1}x_{2}|\leq|y_{1}-x_{1}||y_{2}|+|x_{1}||y_{2}-x_{2}|$ and Lemmas [4](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#Thmtheorem4) and [6](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#Thmtheorem6), it suffices to set $r=0$ and look at the term

| $$n^{-3/4}\sum_{j=0}^{j_{n}(p)-1}\sum_{u=0}^{2(p+1)k_{n}-1}\mathbb{E}\left[\eta(q)_{a_{j}(p)+u}^{n}-\eta(q)_{a_{j}(p)+u,a_{j}(p)}^{n}\mid\mathcal{H}_{a_{j}(p)}^{n}\right].$$ |
| --- |

Now, take $f(x)=|x|^{q}$ and recall that $\alpha_{i,m}^{n}=n^{1/4}\left(\bar{r}_{i}^{*}-\bar{r}_{i,m}^{*}\right)$ . By Taylor’s theorem and Lemmas [4](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#Thmtheorem4) and [6](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#Thmtheorem6), we can reduce the problem to studying the term

| $$n^{-3/4}\sum_{j=0}^{j_{n}(p)-1}\sum_{u=0}^{2(p+1)k_{n}-1}\mathbb{E}\left[f^{\prime}(\eta(q)_{a_{j}(p)+u,a_{j}(p)})\alpha_{a_{j}(p)+u,a_{j}(p)}^{n}\mid\mathcal{H}_{a_{j}(p)}^{n}\right].$$ | (53) |
| --- | --- |

At this stage, we need an extra decomposition:

| $$\alpha_{i,m}^{n}=\alpha_{i,m}^{n}(1)+\alpha_{i,m}^{n}(2),$$ |
| --- |

where

| $\displaystyle\alpha_{i,m}^{n}(1)$ | $\displaystyle=n^{1/4}\sum_{j=1}^{k_{n}-1}g(j/k_{n})\left[\Delta_{n}a_{m\Delta_{n}}+\int_{(i+j-1)\Delta_{n}}^{(i+j)\Delta_{n}}\big[\tilde{\sigma}_{m\Delta_{n}}(W_{s}-W_{m\Delta_{n}})+\tilde{v}_{m\Delta_{n}}(B_{s}-B_{m\Delta_{n}})\big]\mathrm{d}W_{s}\right]$ |
| --- | --- |
| $\displaystyle+n^{1/4}\sum_{j=0}^{k_{n}-1}\big[g(j/k_{n})-g(j+1/k_{n})\big]\left[\bar{\sigma}_{m\Delta_{n}}(W_{(i+j)\Delta_{n}}-W_{m\Delta_{n}})+\bar{v}_{m\Delta_{n}}(B_{(i+j)\Delta_{n}}-B_{m\Delta_{n}})\right]\pi_{i+j},$ |
| $\displaystyle\alpha_{i,m}^{n}(2)$ | $\displaystyle=n^{1/4}\sum_{j=1}^{k_{n}-1}g(j/k_{n})\left[\int_{(i+j-1)\Delta_{n}}^{(i+j)\Delta_{n}}(a_{s}-a_{m\Delta_{n}})\mathrm{d}s+\int_{(i+j-1)\Delta_{n}}^{(i+j)\Delta_{n}}\int_{m\Delta_{n}}^{s}\tilde{a}_{u}\mathrm{d}u\mathrm{d}W_{s}\right]$ |
| $\displaystyle+n^{1/4}\sum_{j=1}^{k_{n}-1}g(j/k_{n})\left[\int_{(i+j-1)\Delta_{n}}^{(i+j)\Delta_{n}}\left[\int_{m\Delta_{n}}^{s}(\tilde{\sigma}_{u}-\tilde{\sigma}_{m\Delta_{n}})\mathrm{d}W_{u}+\int_{m\Delta_{n}}^{s}(\tilde{v}_{u}-\tilde{v}_{m\Delta_{n}})\mathrm{d}B_{u}\right]\mathrm{d}W_{s}\right]$ |
| $\displaystyle+n^{1/4}\sum_{j=0}^{k_{n}-1}\big[g(j/k_{n})-g(j+1/k_{n})\big]\left[\int_{m\Delta_{n}}^{(i+j)\Delta_{n}}\bar{a}_{s}\text{d}s+(\bar{\sigma}_{s}-\bar{\sigma}_{m\Delta_{n}})\mathrm{d}W_{s}+(\bar{v}_{s}-\bar{v}_{m\Delta_{n}})\mathrm{d}B_{s}\right]\pi_{i+j}.$ |

Returning to ([53](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#A1.E53)), we first note that

| $$\mathbb{E}\left[f^{\prime}(\eta(q)_{a_{j}(p)+u,a_{j}(p)})\alpha_{a_{j}(p)+u,a_{j}(p)}^{n}(1)\mid\mathcal{H}_{a_{j}(p)}^{n}\right]=0,$$ |
| --- |

because $(W,B,\pi)\overset{d}{=}-(W,B,\pi)$ , and since $f^{\prime}(\eta(q)_{a_{j}(p)+u,a_{j}(p)})$ is odd and $\alpha_{a_{j}(p)+u,a_{j}(p)}^{n}(1)$ is even in $(W,B,\pi)$ . Furthermore,

| $$\mathbb{E}\left[\big(\alpha_{a_{j}(p)+u,a_{j}(p)}^{n}(2)\big)^{2}\right]\leq C(pk_{n}\Delta_{n})^{2}+Cpn^{-1/2}\mathbb{E}\left[(\gamma_{j}^{n}(p))^{2}\right],$$ |
| --- |

where

| $$\gamma_{j}^{n}(p)=\sup_{a_{j}(p)\Delta_{n}\leq s\leq a_{j+1}(p)\Delta_{n}}\Big[|a_{s}-a_{a_{j(p)}}|+|\tilde{\sigma}_{s}-\tilde{\sigma}_{a_{j(p)}}|+|\tilde{v}_{s}-\tilde{v}_{a_{j(p)}}|+|\bar{\sigma}_{s}-\bar{\sigma}_{a_{j(p)}}|+|\bar{v}_{s}-\bar{v}_{a_{j(p)}}|\Big].$$ |
| --- |

Then, for any fixed $p\geq 2$ , it follows from the Cauchy-Schwarz inequality that

| $\displaystyle\mathbb{E}\big[|A_{1}^{n}|\big]$ | $\displaystyle\leq Cn^{-3/4}\sum_{j=0}^{j_{n}(p)-1}\sum_{u=0}^{2(p+1)k_{n}-1}\mathbb{E}\left[\big(\alpha_{a_{j}(p)+u,a_{j}(p)}^{n}(2)\big)^{2}\right]^{1/2}$ |
| --- | --- |
| $\displaystyle\leq Cn^{-3/4}n(pk_{n}\Delta_{n})+Cpn^{-1/2}\sum_{j=0}^{j_{n}(p)-1}\mathbb{E}\big[(\gamma_{j}^{n}(p))^{2}\big]^{1/2}\rightarrow 0,$ |

as $n\rightarrow\infty$ , which follows from Lemma 5.4 in Jacod, Li, Mykland, Podolskij, and Vetter ([2009](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#bib.bib68)).

Now, we proceed to the last term, $B(p,q,r)^{n}$ . This can be further divided into

| $$B(p,q,r)^{n}=B(p,q,r)_{1}^{n}+B(p,q,r)_{2}^{n},$$ |
| --- |

with

| $\displaystyle B(p,q,r)_{1}^{n}$ | $\displaystyle=n^{1/4}\frac{1}{n}\sum_{j=0}^{j_{n}(p)-1}\sum_{u=0}^{2(p+1)k_{n}-1}\mathbb{E}\left[\eta(q,r)_{a_{j}(p)+u,a_{j}(p)}\mid\mathcal{H}_{a_{j}(p)}^{n}\right]-n^{1/4}\mu_{q}\mu_{r}\sum_{j=0}^{j_{n}(p)-1}\int_{a_{j}(p)\Delta_{n}}^{a_{j+1}(p)\Delta_{n}}\upsilon_{a_{j}(p)}\mathrm{d}t,$ |
| --- | --- |
| $\displaystyle B(p,q,r)_{2}^{n}$ | $\displaystyle=n^{1/4}\mu_{q}\mu_{r}\sum_{j=0}^{j_{n}(p)-1}\int_{a_{j}(p)\Delta_{n}}^{a_{j+1}(p)\Delta_{n}}\big(\upsilon_{a_{j}(p)}-\upsilon_{t}\big)\mathrm{d}t-n^{1/4}\mu_{q}\mu_{r}\int_{i_{n}(p)\Delta_{n}}^{1}\upsilon_{t}\mathrm{d}t,$ |

and where $\upsilon_{t}=\theta\psi_{2}\sigma_{t}^{2}+\frac{1}{\theta}\psi_{1}\rho^{2}\omega_{t}^{2}$ .

In view of Lemma [5](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#Thmtheorem5),

| $$\lim_{p\rightarrow\infty}\limsup_{n\rightarrow\infty}\mathbb{P}\big[|B(p,q,r)_{1}^{n}|>\delta|\big]=0.$$ |
| --- |

Concerning the term $B(p,q,r)_{2}^{n}$ , we remark that $\upsilon_{t}$ is a continuous Itô process due to Assumptions (V) and (N). Hence, a known result on the error of Riemann integration (see, e.g., the proof of Lemma A.1 (iv) in Christensen, Podolskij, Thamrongrat, and Veliyev ([2017](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#bib.bib44))) states that

| $$\mathbb{E}\left[|B(p,q,r)_{2}^{n}|\right]\leq Cn^{1/4}(pk_{n}\Delta_{n}+pn^{-1/2})\leq Cpn^{-1/4},$$ |
| --- |

which completes the proof. $\blacksquare$

###### Lemma 10.

Let $p\geq 2$ be fixed. Under the maintained assumptions of Theorem [3](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#Thmtheorem3), it holds that

| $$n^{1/4}(M(p,q_{1},r_{1})^{n},M(p,q_{2},r_{2})^{n})\overset{\mathcal{D}_{s}}{\longrightarrow}MN(0,\Sigma(p)),$$ |
| --- |

where the $(i,j)$ -th component of the 2x2 covariance matrix $\Sigma(p)$ is defined as

| $$\Sigma(p)_{ij}=\theta\frac{p}{p+1}\int_{0}^{1}\int_{0}^{2}\left(2-\frac{s}{p}\right)h_{ij}\big((\rho\omega_{u},\sigma_{u}),(\psi_{1}/\theta,\theta\psi_{2}),f(s)\big)\mathrm{d}s\mathrm{d}u.$$ |
| --- |

We set $\zeta(p)_{j}^{n}=\big(\zeta(p,q_{1},r_{1},1)_{j}^{n},\zeta(p,q_{2},r_{2},1)_{j}^{n}\big)^{\top}$ for notational convenience. In view of Theorem IX.7.28 in Jacod and Shiryaev ([2003](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#bib.bib71)), it suffices to verify the following four conditions:

| $\displaystyle n^{-1/2}\sum_{j=0}^{j_{n}(p)-1}\mathbb{E}\left[\zeta(p)_{j}^{n}(\zeta(p)_{j}^{n})^{\top}\mid\mathcal{H}_{a_{j}(p)}^{n}\right]\overset{p}{\longrightarrow}\Sigma(p),$ |
| --- |
| $\displaystyle n^{-1/2}\sum_{j=0}^{j_{n}(p)-1}\mathbb{E}\left[\lVert\zeta(p)_{j}^{n}\rVert^{4}\mid\mathcal{H}_{a_{j}(p)}^{n}\right]\overset{p}{\longrightarrow}0,$ |
| $\displaystyle n^{-1/2}\sum_{j=0}^{j_{n}(p)-1}\mathbb{E}\left[\zeta(p)_{j}^{n}(W_{b_{j}(p)\Delta_{n}}-W_{a_{j}(p)\Delta_{n}})\mid\mathcal{H}_{a_{j}(p)}^{n}\right]\overset{p}{\longrightarrow}0,$ |
| $\displaystyle n^{-1/2}\sum_{j=0}^{j_{n}(p)-1}\mathbb{E}\left[\zeta(p)_{j}^{n}(N_{b_{j}(p)\Delta_{n}}-N_{a_{j}(p)\Delta_{n}})\mid\mathcal{H}_{a_{j}(p)}^{n}\right]\overset{p}{\longrightarrow}0,$ |

for any bounded martingale $N$ that is orthogonal to $W$ .

The last three convergences are omitted, since they can be proved exactly as in Podolskij and Vetter ([2009a](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#bib.bib97)) and Jacod, Li, Mykland, Podolskij, and Vetter ([2009](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#bib.bib68)). So we only prove the convergence in the first condition, which is done separately for each component of $\tilde{\Sigma}$ . We only spell out the details for the first term.

To this end, for any $u,v\in A_{j}(p)$ and $u\leq v$ , we note that

| $$\mathbb{E}\left[\tilde{Y}_{u}^{n}\tilde{Y}_{v}^{n}\mid\mathcal{H}_{a_{j}(p)}^{n}\right]=\frac{1}{n}\left(\mathbb{E}\big[\eta_{u,a_{j}(p)}^{n}\eta_{v,a_{j}(p)}^{n}\mid\mathcal{H}_{a_{j}(p)}^{n}\big]-\mathbb{E}\big[\eta_{u,a_{j}(p)}^{n}\mid\mathcal{H}_{a_{j}(p)}^{n}\big]\mathbb{E}\big[\eta_{v,a_{j}(p)}^{n}\mid\mathcal{H}_{a_{j}(p)}^{n}\big]\right).$$ |
| --- |

We suppress the dependence on $q$ and $r$ for brevity. Let $\tilde{\eta}_{i,m}^{n}$ be a version of the variable $\eta_{i,m}^{n}$ , where the noise variable $u$ is replaced by a Gaussian distributed one. In view of Lemma [5](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#Thmtheorem5),

| $$\mathbb{E}\left[\eta_{u,a_{j}(p)}^{n}\mid\mathcal{H}_{a_{j}(p)}^{n}\right]=\mathbb{E}\left[\tilde{\eta}_{u,a_{j}(p)}^{n}\mid\mathcal{H}_{a_{j}(p)}^{n}\right]+o_{p}(n^{-1/4}).$$ |
| --- |

This, together with Lemma [4](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#Thmtheorem4) and the Cauchy-Schwarz inequality, implies that

| $\displaystyle\left|\mathbb{E}\left[\eta_{u,a_{j}(p)}^{n}\eta_{v,a_{j}(p)}^{n}\mid\mathcal{F}_{a_{j}(p)\Delta_{n}}\right]-\mathbb{E}\left[\tilde{\eta}_{u,a_{j}(p)}^{n}\tilde{\eta}_{v,a_{j}(p)}^{n}\mid\mathcal{H}_{a_{j}(p)}^{n}\right]\right|$ | $\displaystyle\leq\left|\mathbb{E}\left[(\eta_{u,a_{j}(p)}^{n}-\tilde{\eta}_{u,a_{j}(p)}^{n})\eta_{v,a_{j}(p)}^{n}\mid\mathcal{H}_{a_{j}(p)}^{n}\right]\right|$ |
| --- | --- |
| $\displaystyle+\left|\mathbb{E}\left[(\eta_{v,a_{j}(p)}^{n}-\tilde{\eta}_{v,a_{j}(p)}^{n})\tilde{\eta}_{u,a_{j}(p)}^{n}\mid\mathcal{H}_{a_{j}(p)}^{n}\right]\right|$ |
| $\displaystyle=o_{p}(n^{-1/8}).$ |

Further, recalling the notation in ([44](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#A1.E44)), we have that

| $\displaystyle\mathbb{E}\left[\tilde{\eta}_{u,a_{j}(p)}^{n}\tilde{\eta}_{v,a_{j}(p)}^{n}\mid\mathcal{H}_{a_{j}(p)}^{n}\right]$ | $\displaystyle-\mathbb{E}\left[\tilde{\eta}_{u,a_{j}(p)}^{n}\mid\mathcal{H}_{a_{j}(p)}^{n}\right]\mathbb{E}\left[\tilde{\eta}_{v,a_{j}(p)}^{n}\mid\mathcal{H}_{a_{j}(p)}^{n}\right]$ |
| --- | --- |
| $\displaystyle=h_{11}\big((\omega_{a_{j}(p)\Delta_{n}},\sigma_{a_{j}(p)\Delta_{n}}),\psi^{n},f^{n}((v-u)/k_{n})\big),$ |

where $\psi^{n}=(n^{1/2}\rho^{2}\psi_{1}^{n}/k_{n},k_{n}\psi_{2}^{n}/n^{1/2})$ and

| $$f^{n}(s)=\bigg(\sum_{m=-\ell}^{\ell}\rho(|m|)f_{1}^{n}(s+m/k_{n}),f_{2}^{n}(s),\sum_{m=-\ell}^{\ell}\rho(|m|)f_{3}^{n}(s+m/k_{n}),f_{4}^{n}(s)\bigg)$$ |
| --- |

with

| $\displaystyle f_{1}^{n}(s)$ | $\displaystyle=n^{1/2}\sum_{j=1}^{k_{n}(1-s)}\left(g\left(\frac{j}{k_{n}}\right)-g\left(\frac{j-1}{k_{n}}\right)\right)\left(g\left(\frac{j+sk_{n}}{k_{n}}\right)-g\left(\frac{j-1+sk_{n}}{k_{n}}\right)\right),$ |
| --- | --- |
| $\displaystyle f_{2}^{n}(s)$ | $\displaystyle=n^{-1/2}\sum_{j=0}^{k_{n}(1-s)}g\left(\frac{j}{k_{n}}\right)g\left(\frac{j+sk_{n}}{k_{n}}\right),$ |
| $\displaystyle f_{3}^{n}(s)$ | $\displaystyle=n^{1/2}\sum_{j=1}^{k_{n}(2-s)}\left(g\left(\frac{j}{k_{n}}\right)-g\left(\frac{j-1}{k_{n}}\right)\right)\left(g\left(\frac{j+sk_{n}-k_{n}}{k_{n}}\right)-g\left(\frac{j-1+sk_{n}-k_{n}}{k_{n}}\right)\right),$ |
| $\displaystyle f_{4}^{n}(s)$ | $\displaystyle=n^{-1/2}\sum_{j=0}^{k_{n}(2-s)}g\left(\frac{j}{k_{n}}\right)g\left(\frac{j+sk_{n}-k_{n}}{k_{n}}\right).$ |

As a consequence, for $u,v\in A_{j}(p)$ and $0\leq v-u<2k_{n}$ , we obtain

| $$\mathbb{E}\left[\tilde{Y}_{u}^{n}\tilde{Y}_{v}^{n}\mid\mathcal{H}_{a_{j}(p)}^{n}\right]=\frac{1}{n}h_{11}\Big((\omega_{a_{j}(p)\Delta_{n}},f^{n}((v-u)/k_{n})\Big)+o_{p}(n^{-1}),$$ |
| --- |

while this term vanishes for $v-u\geq 2k_{n}$ . In turn, this implies that

| $$\mathbb{E}\left[(\zeta(p,1)_{j}^{n})^{2}\mid\mathcal{H}_{a_{j}(p)}^{n}\right]=R_{1}^{n}+R_{2}^{n},$$ |
| --- |

where

| $\displaystyle R_{1}^{n}$ | $\displaystyle=2\sum_{u=a_{j}(p)}^{b_{j}(p)-2k_{n}-1}\sum_{v=u}^{u+2k_{n}-1}\mathbb{E}\left[\tilde{Y}_{u}^{n}\tilde{Y}_{v}^{n}\mid\mathcal{H}_{a_{j}(p)}^{n}\right]-\sum_{u=a_{j}(p)}^{b_{j}(p)-2k_{n}-1}\mathbb{E}\left[(\tilde{Y}_{u}^{n})^{2}\mid\mathcal{H}_{a_{j}(p)}^{n}\right],$ |
| --- | --- |
| $\displaystyle R_{2}^{n}$ | $\displaystyle=2\sum_{u=b_{j}(p)-2k_{n}}^{b_{j}(p)-1}\sum_{v=u}^{b_{j}(p)-1}\mathbb{E}\left[\tilde{Y}_{u}^{n}\tilde{Y}_{v}^{n}\mid\mathcal{H}_{a_{j}(p)}^{n}\right]-\sum_{u=b_{j}(p)-2k_{n}}^{b_{j}(p)-1}\mathbb{E}\left[(\tilde{Y}_{u}^{n})^{2}\mid\mathcal{H}_{a_{j}(p)}^{n}\right].$ |

Consider the first term,

| $\displaystyle n^{-1/2}\sum_{j=0}^{j_{n}(p)-1}R_{1}^{n}$ | $\displaystyle=n^{-3/2}2(2pk_{n}-2k_{n})\sum_{j=0}^{j_{n}(p)-1}\sum_{v=0}^{2k_{n}-1}h_{11}\big((\omega_{a_{j}(p)\Delta_{n}},\sigma_{a_{j}(p)\Delta_{n}}),\psi^{n},f^{n}(v/k_{n})\big)+o_{p}(1)$ |
| --- | --- |
| $\displaystyle\overset{p}{\longrightarrow}2\theta\frac{p-1}{p+1}\int_{0}^{1}\int_{0}^{2}h_{11}\big((\rho\omega_{u},\sigma_{u}),(\psi_{1}/\theta,\theta\psi_{2}),f(s)\big)\mathrm{d}s\mathrm{d}u,$ |

where the argument is as in Podolskij and Vetter ([2009a](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#bib.bib97)) via continuity of the function $h_{11}$ and Lebesgue’s theorem.

Furthermore, we deduce that

| $\displaystyle n^{-1/2}\sum_{j=0}^{j_{n}(p)-1}n^{-1/2}R_{2}^{n}$ | $\displaystyle=n^{-3/2}2\sum_{j=0}^{j_{n}(p)-1}\sum_{v=0}^{2k_{n}-1}(2k_{n}-v)h_{11}\big((\omega_{a_{j}(p)\Delta_{n}},\sigma_{a_{j}(p)\Delta_{n}}),\psi^{n},f^{n}(v/k_{n})\big)+o_{p}(1)$ |
| --- | --- |
| $\displaystyle\overset{p}{\longrightarrow}\frac{\theta}{p+1}\int_{0}^{1}\int_{0}^{2}(2-s)h_{11}\big((\rho\omega_{u},\sigma_{u}),(\psi_{1}/\theta,\theta\psi_{2}),f(s)\big)\mathrm{d}s\mathrm{d}u.$ |

The statement then follows by summing these two terms. $\blacksquare$ The proof of Theorem [3](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#Thmtheorem3) now follows directly from ([50](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#A1.E50)) and Lemmas [7](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#Thmtheorem7)– [10](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#Thmtheorem10).

### Proof of Theorem 1

The proof is mostly based on Theorem [3](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#Thmtheorem3) above, after we take into account that the error in the noise variance estimator introduced in ([12](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#S2.E12)). We recall that the long-run variance of the process $\pi$ is defined as $\rho^{2}=\rho(0)+2\sum_{m=1}^{\ell}\rho(m)$ , where $\rho(m)=\mathbb{E}[\pi_{0}\pi_{m}]$ is the $m$ th autocovariance.

###### Lemma 11.

Assume that $r$ follows the process in ([3](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#S2.E3)) and that Assumptions (V) and (N) hold. Then, as $n\rightarrow\infty$ , such that $h_{n}\asymp n^{1/5}$ and $\ell_{n}\asymp n^{1/8}$ , it holds that

| $$\hat{\omega}_{n}^{2}-\int_{0}^{1}\rho^{2}\omega_{s}^{2}\mathrm{d}s=o_{p}(n^{-1/4}).$$ | (54) |
| --- | --- |

For each $h\geq 0$ , we observe that

| $\displaystyle p_{i\Delta_{n}}^{*}-\tilde{p}_{(i+h)\Delta_{n}}^{*}=\alpha_{i}^{n}(h)+\beta_{i}^{n}(h),$ |
| --- |

with

| $$\alpha_{i}^{n}(h)=\omega_{i\Delta_{n}}(\pi_{i}-\tilde{\pi}_{i+h})\quad\text{and}\quad\beta_{i}^{n}(h)=p_{i\Delta_{n}}-\tilde{p}_{(i+h)\Delta_{n}}+\frac{1}{h_{n}}\sum_{j=0}^{h_{n}-1}\pi_{i+h+j}(\omega_{i\Delta_{n}}-\omega_{(i+h+j)\Delta_{n}}).$$ |
| --- |

It suffices to prove that for each $m\in\{0,\dots,\ell_{n}\}$ :

| $$\rho_{n}(m)-\int_{0}^{1}\rho(m)\omega_{s}^{2}\mathrm{d}s=O_{p}\left(n^{-2/5}\right).$$ |
| --- |

Then, based on the required order of $h_{n}$ and $\ell_{n}$ , summing up these terms results in an $O_{p}(n^{-11/40})$ bound on the error of $\hat{\omega}_{n}^{2}$ .

To show this result, we suppose (without loss of generality) that $n$ is chosen so large that $h_{n}>\ell$ and note that $\rho(k)=0$ for $k>\ell$ . We proceed with the following decomposition:

| $$\rho_{n}(m)-\int_{0}^{1}\rho(m)\omega_{s}^{2}\mathrm{d}s=N_{1}^{n}(m)+N_{2}^{n}(m)+N_{3}^{n}(m),$$ |
| --- |

where

| $\displaystyle N_{1}^{n}(m)$ | $\displaystyle=\frac{1}{n-5h_{n}+1}\sum_{i=0}^{n-5h_{n}}\omega_{i\Delta_{n}}\omega_{(i+m)\Delta_{n}}\big[(\pi_{i}-\tilde{\pi}_{i+2h_{n}})(\pi_{i+m}-\tilde{\pi}_{i+4h_{n}})-\rho(m)\big],$ |
| --- | --- |
| $\displaystyle N_{2}^{n}(m)$ | $\displaystyle=-\rho(m)\int_{(n-5h_{n}+1)\Delta_{n}}^{1}\omega_{s}^{2}\mathrm{d}s+\frac{\rho(m)}{n-5h_{n}+1}\sum_{i=0}^{n-5h_{n}}\int_{i\Delta_{n}}^{(i+1)\Delta_{n}}\left(\omega_{i\Delta_{n}}\omega_{(i+m)\Delta_{n}}-\omega_{s}^{2}\right)\mathrm{d}s,$ |
| $\displaystyle N_{3}^{n}(m)$ | $\displaystyle=\frac{1}{n-5h_{n}+1}\sum_{i=0}^{n-5h_{n}}\Big[\alpha_{i}^{n}(2h_{n})\beta_{i+m}^{n}(4h_{n}-m)+\alpha_{i+m}^{n}(4h_{n}-m)\beta_{i+m}^{n}(4h_{n})$ |
| $\displaystyle\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\ \,+\beta_{i}^{n}(2h_{n})\beta_{i+m}^{n}(4h_{n}-m)\Big].$ |

We proceed to show that each of these terms is asymptotically negligible. In view of (2.1.44) in Jacod and Protter ([2012](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#bib.bib70)), we deduce that $\sup_{0\leq i\leq n-h}\mathbb{E}\left[(p_{i\Delta_{n}}-p_{(i+h)\Delta_{n}})^{2}\right]\leq Ch/n.$ This order also holds if $p$ is replaced by $\omega$ . Then, combining these bounds with $\mathbb{E}[\pi_{i}^{2}]\leq C$ yields:

| $$\mathbb{E}\left[\alpha_{i}^{n}(h)^{2}\right]\leq C\quad\text{and}\quad\mathbb{E}\left[\beta_{i}^{n}(h)^{2}\right]\leq C\frac{h}{n}.$$ |
| --- |

Consequently,

| $$\mathbb{E}\left[|N_{n}^{3}(m)|\right]\leq C\sqrt{\frac{h_{n}}{n}}\leq Cn^{-2/5}.$$ |
| --- |

Next, we deal with the term $N_{2}^{n}(m)$ , which is non-zero for $m\leq\ell$ , which we suppose is true. A standard Riemann integration error argument then yields $N_{2}^{n}(m)=O_{p}(n^{-1/2})$ .

Concerning the term $N_{1}^{n}(m)$ , due to Assumption (N,i+iv) the summands are correlated only up to order $\ell_{n}$ , and hence

| $$\mathbb{E}\left[(N_{n}^{1}(m))^{2}\right]\leq C\frac{\ell_{n}}{n},$$ |
| --- |

so $N_{1}^{n}(m)=O_{p}\big((\ell_{n}/n)^{1/2}\big)$ . Adding up terms leads to the statement in ([54](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#A1.E54)). $\blacksquare$

We now turn to the proof of Theorem [1](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#Thmtheorem1). For the convergence in probability part it follows from the above results that

| $\displaystyle BV_{n}(2,0)$ | $\displaystyle\overset{p}{\longrightarrow}\int_{0}^{1}\Big(\theta\psi_{2}\sigma_{s}^{2}+\frac{1}{\theta}\psi_{1}\rho^{2}\omega_{s}^{2}\Big)\mathrm{d}s+\theta\psi_{2}\sum_{0\leq s\leq 1}(\Delta r_{s})^{2},$ |
| --- | --- |
| $\displaystyle BV_{n}(1,1)$ | $\displaystyle\overset{p}{\longrightarrow}\int_{0}^{1}\Big(\theta\psi_{2}\sigma_{s}^{2}+\frac{1}{\theta}\psi_{1}\rho^{2}\omega_{s}^{2}\Big)\mathrm{d}s.$ |

This can be shown by proceeding as in the proof of Theorem 2 in Podolskij and Vetter ([2009a](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#bib.bib97)). Now, the consistency in Theorem [1](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#Thmtheorem1) follows from Lemma [11](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#Thmtheorem11). The central limit theorem is derived under the further assumption that $p$ is continuous. Hence, it follows from Theorem [3](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#Thmtheorem3) [choosing the exponents $(q_{1},r_{1})=(2,0)$ and $(q_{2},r_{2})=(1,1)$ ] and Lemma [11](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#Thmtheorem11). $\blacksquare$

### A.2 A jump- and noise-robust estimator of $\tilde{\Sigma}$

Here, we present an estimator of $\tilde{\Sigma}$ , which appears in the extended central limit theorem in Theorem [3](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#Thmtheorem3). Its connection with $\Sigma$ is explained in ([46](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#A1.E46)).

We build on previous work of Christensen, Podolskij, Thamrongrat, and Veliyev ([2017](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#bib.bib44)), who propose a subsampling estimator of $\tilde{\Sigma}$ (see also Politis, Romano, and Wolf, [1999](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#bib.bib99); Kalnina, [2011](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#bib.bib76); Mykland and Zhang, [2017](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#bib.bib92)). An appealing feature of their estimator is that it is positive semi-definite and has good small sample properties.666666Podolskij and Vetter ([2009a](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#bib.bib97)) develop an element-by-element estimator of $\tilde{\Sigma}$ in the i.i.d noise setting. However, this estimator is not positive semi-definite and is often ill-conditioned in practice. Moreover, it is not consistent for $\tilde{\Sigma}$ under the jump alternative. In that paper, the subsampling estimator is shown to be consistent if there is either price jumps or microstructure noise, but not in the presence of both. Microstructure noise is further restricted to be either heteroscedastic or dependent, but not both. Here, we extend their framework to account for all of these features at once.

We propose the following jump- and noise-robust covariance matrix estimator:

| $$\tilde{\Sigma}_{n}=\frac{1}{L}\sum_{l=1}^{L}\Bigg(\frac{n^{1/4}}{\sqrt{L}}\begin{bmatrix}\check{BV}_{l}(q_{1},r_{1})-\check{BV}_{n}(q_{1},r_{1})\\[2.84544pt] \check{BV}_{l}(q_{2},r_{2})-\check{BV}_{n}(q_{2},r_{2})\end{bmatrix}\Bigg)\Bigg(\frac{n^{1/4}}{\sqrt{L}}\begin{bmatrix}\check{BV}_{l}(q_{1},r_{1})-\check{BV}_{n}(q_{1},r_{1})\\[2.84544pt] \check{BV}_{l}(q_{2},r_{2})-\check{BV}_{n}(q_{2},r_{2})\end{bmatrix}\Bigg)^{\top},$$ |
| --- |

with

| $\displaystyle\begin{split}\check{BV}_{n}(q,r)&=\frac{1}{n}\sum_{i=0}^{n-2k_{n}+1}|n^{1/4}\check{r}_{i}^{*}|^{q}|n^{1/4}\check{r}_{i+k_{n}}^{*}|^{r},\qquad\check{BV}_{l}(q,r)=\frac{Lpk_{n}}{n}\sum_{i=1}^{n/Lpk_{n}}v_{(i-1)L+l}(q,r)^{n},\\[2.84544pt] v_{i}(q,r)^{n}&=\frac{1}{pk_{n}-2k_{n}+2}\sum_{j,j+2k_{n}-1\in B_{i}(p)}|n^{1/4}\check{r}_{j}^{*}|^{q}|n^{1/4}\check{r}_{j+k_{n}}^{*}|^{r},\end{split}$ |
| --- |

and

| $$\check{r}_{i}^{*}=\bar{r}_{i}^{*}\mathbbm{1}(|\bar{r}_{i}^{*}|\leq u_{n}),$$ | (55) |
| --- | --- |

where $\mathbbm{1}(A)$ is an indicator function that equals one if $A$ is true, zero otherwise, and $u_{n}=\alpha n^{-\bar{\omega}}$ with $\alpha>0$ and $\bar{\omega}\in(0,1/4)$ .

In the main text, we present an estimator of $\Sigma$ , $\Sigma_{n}^{*}$ , for the particular setting with $(q_{1},r_{1})=(2,0)$ and $(q_{2},r_{2})=(1,1)$ . Its connection with $\tilde{\Sigma}_{n}$ is given by:

| $$\Sigma_{n,ij}^{*}=\left(\sqrt{n}c_{1}^{n}\right)^{2}\left(\frac{\pi}{2}\right)^{i+j-2}\tilde{\Sigma}_{n,ij}.$$ |
| --- |

To deal with microstructure noise, $\tilde{\Sigma}_{n}$ is based on the pre-averaged bipower variation. To further robustify it to jumps, we exploit the truncation device of Mancini ([2009](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#bib.bib88)) in ([55](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#A1.E55)). This sets large negative or positive pre-averaged log-returns to zero, since they are most likely dominated by the jump component. The threshold, $u_{n}$ , is adapted to an estimate of the square-root integrated variance by setting $\alpha=c\sqrt{BV_{n}(1,1)}$ . With this configuration, we can interpret $c$ as the number of local diffusive standard deviations a pre-averaged log-return must exceed to be labelled a jump. The rate parameter, $\bar{\omega}$ , ensures that, asymptotically, continuous pre-averaged log-returns are unaffected by the truncation. In this way, $\tilde{\Sigma}_{n}$ is also made jump-robust.

###### Theorem 12.

Assume that $r$ follows ([3](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#S2.E3)) with $\beta\leq 1$ and that Assumptions (V) and (N) hold. Moreover, for each $s\in\{q_{1},q_{2},r_{1},r_{2}\}\cap[1,\infty)$ , we require that

| $$\frac{q+\delta-1/2}{4q-\beta}<\bar{\omega}<\frac{1}{4}-\delta$$ |
| --- |

where $L\asymp n^{(1-\delta)/2}$ . Then, as $n\rightarrow\infty$ , $\rightarrow\infty$ , $L/p\rightarrow\infty$ , $\sqrt{n}/Lp^{2}\rightarrow\infty$ and $\delta<1/16$ , it holds that

| $$\tilde{\Sigma}_{n}\overset{p}{\longrightarrow}\tilde{\Sigma}.$$ |
| --- |

### Proof of Theorem 12

By the polarization identity, it suffices to show the result $\tilde{\Sigma}_{n}-\tilde{\Sigma}\overset{p}{\longrightarrow}0$ in the univariate setting. To this end, we denote by $\bar{r}_{i}^{\prime}$ the pre-averaged return based on the continuous part of $p_{t}$ and $\epsilon_{t}$ . The corresponding subsampling estimator is denoted as

| $$\Sigma_{n}^{\prime}=\frac{1}{L}\sum_{l=1}^{L}\Biggl(\frac{n^{1/4}}{\sqrt{L}}\Bigl(BV_{l}^{\prime}(q,r)-BV_{n}^{\prime}(q,r)\Bigr)\Biggr)^{2},$$ |
| --- |

where

| $\displaystyle BV_{n}^{\prime}(q,r)$ | $\displaystyle=\frac{1}{n}\sum_{i=0}^{n-2k_{n}+1}|n^{1/4}\bar{r}_{i}^{\prime}|^{q}|n^{1/4}\bar{r}_{i+k_{n}}^{\prime}|^{r},\qquad BV_{l}^{\prime}(q,r)=\frac{Lpk_{n}}{n}\sum_{i=1}^{n/Lpk_{n}}v^{\prime}_{(i-1)L+l}(q,r)^{n},$ |
| --- | --- |
| $\displaystyle v^{\prime}_{i}(q,r)^{n}$ | $\displaystyle=\frac{1}{pk_{n}-2k_{n}+2}\sum_{j,j+2k_{n}-1\in B_{i}(p)}|n^{1/4}\bar{r}_{j}^{\prime}|^{q}|n^{1/4}\bar{r}_{j+k_{n}}^{\prime}|^{r}.$ |

Proceeding exactly as in the proof of Theorem 3.8 in Christensen, Podolskij, Thamrongrat, and Veliyev ([2017](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#bib.bib44)), it immediately follows that $\Sigma_{n}^{\prime}-\tilde{\Sigma}\overset{p}{\longrightarrow}0$ . Hence, it suffices to show that

| $$\tilde{\Sigma}_{n}-\Sigma_{n}^{\prime}\overset{p}{\longrightarrow}0.$$ |
| --- |

To establish this, we note that:

| $\displaystyle\tilde{\Sigma}_{n}-\Sigma_{n}^{\prime}=\frac{1}{L}\sum_{l=1}^{L}$ | $\displaystyle\Bigg(\frac{n^{1/4}}{\sqrt{L}}\bigg(BV_{l}(q,r)-BV_{l}^{\prime}(q,r)+BV_{n}^{\prime}(q,r)-BV_{n}(q,r)\bigg)\Bigg)$ |
| --- | --- |
| $\displaystyle\times$ | $\displaystyle\Bigg(\frac{n^{1/4}}{\sqrt{L}}\bigg(BV_{l}(q,r)-BV_{n}(q,r)+BV_{l}^{\prime}(q,r)-BV_{n}^{\prime}(q,r)\bigg)\Bigg),$ |

which is an average of $L$ terms. The main idea is to deduce that each of these $L$ terms converges in probability to zero. In particular, we show uniform convergence in mean square:

| $$\sup_{1\leq l\leq L}\mathbb{E}\bigg[\Big|\frac{n^{1/4}}{\sqrt{L}}\big(BV_{l}(q,r)-BV_{l}^{\prime}(q,r)\big)\Big|^{2}\bigg]\rightarrow 0\quad\text{and}\quad\sup_{1\leq l\leq L}\mathbb{E}\bigg[\Big|\frac{n^{1/4}}{\sqrt{L}}\big(BV_{n}(q,r)-BV_{n}^{\prime}(q,r)\big)\Big|^{2}\bigg]\rightarrow 0.$$ | (56) |
| --- | --- |

Following the arguments in the proof of Lemma A.6 in Christensen, Podolskij, Thamrongrat, and Veliyev ([2017](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#bib.bib44)) implies that

| $$\sup_{1\leq l\leq L}\mathbb{E}\left[\Big|\frac{n^{1/4}}{\sqrt{L}}\big(BV_{l}^{\prime}(q,r)-BV_{n}^{\prime}(q,r)\big)\Big|^{2}\right]\leq C.$$ | (57) |
| --- | --- |

Then, equation ([56](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#A1.E56)) – ([57](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#A1.E57)) lead to:

| $$\sup_{1\leq l\leq L}\mathbb{E}\bigg[\Big|\frac{n^{1/4}}{\sqrt{L}}\big(BV_{l}(q,r)-BV_{n}(q,r)\big)\Big|^{2}\bigg]\leq C.$$ |
| --- |

The combination of the last three results and the Cauchy-Schwarz inequality implies:

| $$\mathbb{E}\big[|\tilde{\Sigma}_{n}-\Sigma_{n}^{\prime}|\big]\rightarrow 0,$$ |
| --- |

so that $\tilde{\Sigma}_{n}-\Sigma_{n}^{\prime}\overset{p}{\longrightarrow}0$ . Thus, it is enough to show ([56](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#A1.E56)).

For each $j$ , we define

| $\displaystyle\lambda_{j}^{n}(q,r)=|n^{1/4}\bar{r}_{j}^{*}|^{q}|n^{1/4}\bar{r}_{j+k_{n}}^{*}|^{r}\mathbbm{1}_{\{|\bar{r}_{j}^{*}|\leq u_{n}\cap|\bar{r}_{j+k_{n}}^{*}|\leq u_{n}\}}-|n^{1/4}\bar{r}_{j}^{\prime}|^{q}|n^{1/4}\bar{r}_{j+k_{n}}^{\prime}|^{r}.$ |
| --- |

Note that

| $$BV_{n}(q,r)-BV_{n}^{\prime}(q,r)=\frac{1}{n}\sum_{i=0}^{n-2k_{n}+1}\lambda_{i}^{n}(q,r),$$ |
| --- |

and

| $$BV_{l}(q,r)-BV_{l}^{\prime}(q,r)=\frac{Lpk_{n}}{n}\frac{1}{pk_{n}-2k_{n}+2}\sum_{i=1}^{n/Lpk_{n}}\sum_{j,j+k_{n}-1\in B_{(i-1)L+l}(p)}\lambda_{j}^{n}(q,r).$$ |
| --- |

As a result of Cauchy-Schwarz, showing ([56](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#A1.E56)) is reduced to proving:

| $$\sup_{1\leq j\leq n-2k_{n}+2}\mathbb{E}\bigg[\Big|\frac{n^{1/4}}{\sqrt{L}}\lambda_{j}^{n}(q,r)\Big|^{2}\bigg]\rightarrow 0.$$ |
| --- |

Furthermore, the identity

| $$\lambda_{j}^{n}(q,r)=\lambda_{j}^{n}(q,0)|n^{1/4}\bar{r}_{j+k_{n}}^{\prime}|^{r}+\lambda_{j}^{n}(0,r)|n^{1/4}\bar{r}_{j}^{\prime}|^{q}+\lambda_{j}^{n}(q,0)\lambda_{j}^{n}(0,r),$$ |
| --- |

and Lemma [4](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#Thmtheorem4) combined with Cauchy-Schwarz inequality yields:

| $$\mathbb{E}\big[|\lambda_{j}^{n}(q,r)|^{2}\big]\leq C\Big(\mathbb{E}\big[|\lambda_{j}^{n}(q,0)|^{4}\big]^{1/2}+\mathbb{E}\big[|\lambda_{j}^{n}(0,r)|^{4}\big]^{1/2}+\mathbb{E}\big[|\lambda_{j}^{n}(q,0)|^{4}\big]^{1/2}\mathbb{E}\big[|\lambda_{j}^{n}(0,r)|^{4}\big]^{1/2}\Big).$$ |
| --- |

Then, in view of the rate condition $n^{1/4}/\sqrt{L}\rightarrow\infty,$ it is enough that

| $$\sup_{1\leq j\leq n-2k_{n}+2}\mathbb{E}\bigg[\Big|\frac{n^{1/4}}{\sqrt{L}}\lambda_{j}^{n}(q,0)\Big|^{4}\bigg]\rightarrow 0\quad\text{and}\quad\sup_{1\leq j\leq n-2k_{n}+2}\mathbb{E}\bigg[\Big|\frac{n^{1/4}}{\sqrt{L}}\lambda_{j}^{n}(0,r)\Big|^{4}\bigg]\rightarrow 0.$$ |
| --- |

We only look at the term $\lambda_{j}^{n}(q,0)$ with $q>0$ , for which

| $$\lambda_{j}^{n}(q,0)=n^{q/4}|\bar{r}_{j}^{\prime}+\bar{r}_{j}^{d}|^{q}\mathbbm{1}_{\{|\bar{r}_{j}^{*}|\leq u_{n}\}}-n^{q/4}|\bar{r}_{j}^{\prime}|^{q}.$$ |
| --- |

Now, for any $u>0$ , $a\geq 0$ and $b\geq 0$ , it holds that

| $$\big||x+y|^{q}\mathbbm{1}_{\{|x+y|\leq u\}}-|x|^{q}\big|\leq C\bigg(\frac{|x|^{q+a}}{u^{a}}+|x|^{q}\frac{|y|^{b}}{u^{b}}+(|y|\wedge u)^{q}+\mathbbm{1}_{\{q>1\}}|x|^{q-1}(|y|\wedge u)\bigg),$$ |
| --- |

which can be verified by looking at the cases:

| $$(1)\ |x|\geq u/2,\qquad(2)\ |x| u,\qquad(3)\ |x| 1/4$ . Now, arguing as in Jacod and Protter ([2012](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#bib.bib70), p. 529), we deduce that

| $$\mathbb{E}\left[(|\bar{r}_{j}^{d}|\wedge u_{n})^{2}\right]\leq C\frac{\rho_{n}}{n^{1/2+(2-\beta)\bar{\omega}}}.$$ |
| --- |

In view of the fact that $(|x|\wedge u)^{q}\leq u^{q-2}(|x|\wedge u)^{2}$ for $q\geq 2$ and recalling that $u_{n}=\alpha n^{-\bar{\omega}}$ :

| $$\mathbb{E}\left[(|\bar{r}_{j}^{d}|\wedge u_{n})^{q}\right]\leq C\times\begin{cases}\displaystyle\frac{\rho_{n}^{q/2}}{n^{q/4+(2-\beta)\bar{\omega}q/2}}&\text{if }q\leq 2,\\[2.84544pt] \displaystyle\frac{u_{n}^{q-2}\rho_{n}}{n^{1/2+(2-\beta)\bar{\omega}}}\leq\frac{\rho_{n}}{n^{1/2+(q-\beta)\bar{\omega}}}&\text{if }q>2.\end{cases}$$ |
| --- |

Moreover, for any $q>0$ :

| $$\mathbb{E}\big[|\bar{r}_{j}^{\prime}|^{q}\big]\leq C\frac{1}{n^{q/4}}\quad\text{and}\quad\mathbb{E}\big[|\bar{r}_{j}^{d}|^{2}\big]\leq C\frac{1}{n^{1/2}}.$$ |
| --- |

Combining these inequalities with $a=4$ , $b=1$ and and recalling $L\asymp n^{(1-\delta)/2}$ leads to

| $$\mathbb{E}\bigg[\Big|\frac{n^{1/4}}{\sqrt{L}}\lambda_{j}^{n}(q,0)\Big|^{4}\bigg]\leq C\bigg(\frac{1}{n^{1-\delta-4\bar{\omega}}}+\frac{1}{n^{1/4-\delta-\bar{\omega}}}+\frac{\rho_{n}}{n^{1/2-\delta-q+(4q-\beta)\bar{\omega}}}+\frac{\rho_{n}^{1/2}}{n^{-\delta+(1-\beta/2)\bar{\omega}}}\bigg)$$ |
| --- |

The first and second error terms converge to 0 due to the condition $\bar{\omega}<1/4-\delta$ . The third error term converges to 0 due to the assumption $\bar{\omega}>(q+\delta-1/2)/(4q-\beta)$ . This also implies $\bar{\omega}>1/8$ , which combined with $\beta\leq 1$ and $\delta<1/16$ deals with the fourth term. $\blacksquare$

### Proof of Theorem 2

The result follows directly from the proof of Theorem [12](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#Thmtheorem12) by taking $(q_{1},r_{1})=(2,0)$ , $(q_{2},r_{2})=(1,1)$ and adjusting by the constants $c_{1}$ and $c_{2}$ . It is worth highlighting that the constraint $\bar{\omega}>(q+\delta-1/2)/(4q-\beta)$ is more binding for $q=2$ compared to $q=1$ . $\blacksquare$

### A.3 Irrelevance of truncation

To show that the truncation does not influence the limiting distribution in Theorem [3](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#Thmtheorem3) and, hence, does not affect Theorem [1](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#Thmtheorem1), we note that

| $$n^{1/4}\begin{pmatrix}BV_{n}(q_{1},r_{1})-V(q_{1},r_{1})\\[2.84544pt] BV_{n}(q_{2},r_{2})-V(q_{2},r_{2})\end{pmatrix}=n^{1/4}\begin{pmatrix}BV_{n}(q_{1},r_{1})-V(q_{1},r_{1})\\[2.84544pt] \check{BV}_{n}(q_{2},r_{2})-V(q_{2},r_{2})\end{pmatrix}+n^{1/4}\begin{pmatrix}0\\[2.84544pt] BV_{n}(q_{2},r_{2})-\check{BV}_{n}(q_{2},r_{2})\end{pmatrix}.$$ |
| --- |

It is thus enough to show that $n^{1/4}\big(BV_{n}(q_{2},r_{2})-\check{BV}_{n}(q_{2},r_{2})\big)=o_{p}(1)$ . An application of Boole’s and Markov’s inequalities and Lemma [4](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#Thmtheorem4) in the proof of Theorem [3](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#Thmtheorem3) yields that

| $$\mathbb{P}\big[BV_{n}(q_{2},r_{2})\neq\check{BV}_{n}(q_{2},r_{2})\big]\leq\sum_{i=1}^{n-k_{n}+2}\mathbb{P}\big[|\bar{r}_{i}^{*}|>u_{n}\big]\leq\frac{C}{n^{M(1/4-\bar{\omega})-1}},$$ |
| --- |

where $M>0$ . As $M$ can be chosen arbitrarily large, the result follows. $\blacksquare$

## Appendix B Monte Carlo Simulations

The jump-testing theory in Section [2](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#S2) is derived using an infill asymptotic setup, which assumes that $\Delta_{n}\rightarrow 0$ . This is unattainable in practice. In this appendix, we therefore conduct a series of Monte Carlo simulations to examine the finite sample properties of the test statistic in a more realistic setting.

We simulate from the following model:

| $$r_{t}^{*}=r_{t}+\epsilon_{t},$$ | (58) |
| --- | --- |

where $r_{t}=r_{t}^{c}+r_{t}^{d}$ , and

| $$\text{d}r_{t}^{c}=\sigma_{t}\text{d}W_{t},$$ | (59) |
| --- | --- |

with $r_{0}\equiv 0$ .

The drift term is omitted (i.e., $a_{t}\equiv 0$ ) so the continuous part of $r_{t}$ evolves as a local martingale. The contribution of the drift is small over short horizons and the terms involving it—including cross products—are asymptotically negligible. It therefore tends to play a minor role in the high-frequency setting.676767A notable exception is Christensen, Oomen, and Renò ([2022](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#bib.bib43)). Similarly, adding a realistic expected rate of return does not affect the conclusions in any material way.

The instantaneous variance is assumed to be driven by a square-root process (e.g., Cox, Ingersoll, and Ross, [1985](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#bib.bib46); Heston, [1993](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#bib.bib66)):

| $$\text{d}\sigma_{t}^{2}=\kappa(\sigma^{2}-\sigma_{t}^{2})\text{d}t+\xi\sigma_{t}\text{d}B_{t},$$ | (60) |
| --- | --- |

with $\sigma_{0}^{2}\sim\text{Gamma}(2\kappa\sigma^{2}\xi^{-2},2\kappa\xi^{-2})$ . Our choice of parameters is based on previous work such as Aït-Sahalia and Kimmel ([2007](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#bib.bib6)). Specifically, we set $\kappa=5$ , $\sigma=0.4$ , $\xi=0.5$ , and $\rho=-\sqrt{0.5}$ , where $\rho$ is the leverage correlation between the standard Brownian motions $\mathbb{E}[\text{d}W_{t}\text{d}B_{t}]=\rho\text{d}t$ . This yields a stationary and strictly positive variance in continuous-time (i.e., the Feller condition $\xi^{2}<2\kappa\sigma^{2}$ holds) with an annualized mean volatility of 40%.

$r_{t}^{d}$ is an infinite-activity tempered stable process with Lévy measure

| $$\nu(\text{d}x)=\tau\frac{e^{-\lambda x}}{x^{1+\beta}}\text{d}x,$$ | (61) |
| --- | --- |

where $\tau>0$ and $\lambda>0$ .

The activity of the jump process is controlled by $\beta$ . As $\beta$ increases, the density of the small jumps is enlarged, and the realizations of $r_{t}^{d}$ become more vibrant and start to resemble those of a Brownian motion (see Figure [B.1](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#A2.F1) for an illustration). As noted by Bollerslev and Todorov ([2011](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#bib.bib31)), this renders the decomposition of diffusive and jump risk meaningless in practice and also makes it harder to separate the null from the jump alternative (Aït-Sahalia, Jacod, and Li ([2012](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#bib.bib5)), ). A priori, we therefore expect larger values of $\beta$ to be detrimental to the rejection rate of the jump test statistic under the alternative. To gauge the impact of changing $\beta$ , we follow Aït-Sahalia, Jacod, and Li ([2012](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#bib.bib5)) and examine $\beta=0.50$ , $1.00$ , $1.50$ and $1.75$ . Moreover, we set $\lambda=3$ and calibrate $\tau$ so that $r_{t}^{d}$ induces 20% of the quadratic return variation, on average.

We simulate for $t\in[0,1]$ with a time step of $\Delta_{n}=1/23{,}400$ . This can be interpreted as adding a new observation every second during a 6.5 hour trading session. A standard Euler discretization is employed for the continuous part.686868There is a small probability that the variance in ([60](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#A2.E60)) goes negative in finite samples. We therefore enforce a full truncation floor at zero to avoid this (e.g., Andersen, [2008](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#bib.bib8)). As described in Todorov, Tauchen, and Grynkiv ([2014](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#bib.bib102)), the jump component is computed as the difference between two spectrally positive tempered stable processes that are generated using the acceptance-rejection algorithm of Baeumer and Meerschaert ([2010](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#bib.bib11)). The total number of Monte Carlo replications is $T=10{,}000$ . Figure [B.1](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#A2.F1) illustrates a single sample path of $r_{t}^{c}$ and $r_{t}^{d}$ .

Figure B.1: A single simulation of the efficient log-return.

| Panel A: Sample path of $r_{t}^{c}$ . | Panel B: Sample path of $r_{t}^{d}$ . |
| --- | --- |

Note. We show a realization of the components of the efficient log-return $r_{t}$ , which is a superposition of a continuous sample path Heston ([1993](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#bib.bib66))-type stochastic volatility model ( $r_{t}^{c}$ in Panel A) and a pure jump process of tempered stable-type ( $r_{t}^{d}$ in Panel B). The latter is plotted as a function of the activity index $\beta$ using a common random seed.

We next explain how $r_{t}$ is disrupted with microstructure noise, $\epsilon_{t}$ . In particular, we study four types of additive measurement error:

| $$\epsilon_{t}=\begin{cases}\displaystyle\gamma\sqrt{\text{IV}\Delta_{n}}\epsilon_{t}^{N},&\text{``Gaussian'',}\\[2.84544pt] \displaystyle\gamma\sqrt{\text{IV}\Delta_{n}}\epsilon_{t}^{T}\sqrt{\frac{\eta-2}{\eta}},&\text{``T-distributed'',}\\[2.84544pt] \displaystyle\gamma\sqrt{\text{IV}\Delta_{n}}\frac{\epsilon_{t}^{N}+\phi\epsilon_{t-1}^{N}}{1+\phi^{2}},&\text{``Autocorrelated'',}\\[2.84544pt] \displaystyle\gamma\sigma_{t}\sqrt{\Delta_{n}}\epsilon_{t}^{N},&\text{``Heteroscedastic''}.\end{cases}$$ | (62) |
| --- | --- |

where $\epsilon_{t}^{N}$ and $\epsilon_{t}^{T}$ are i.i.d. sequences of Gaussian and $t$ -distributed (with $\eta$ degrees of freedom) random variables.

While the Gaussian i.i.d. draws are standard, $t$ -distributed noise is less common. The latter, however, can generate notable outliers if $\eta$ is small. This leads to infrequent—but large—bouncebacks in the noisy log-price series which is a common trait of raw high-frequency data. We set $\eta=2.5$ to be consistent with this observation.

We also examine cases with autocorrelated and heteroscedastic noise. The former scenario assumes $\epsilon_{t}$ is MA(1) with degree of memory determined by the parameter $\phi$ which is fixed at $\phi=-0.77$ . Throughout, the variance of the noise (on a per increment basis) changes with the level of volatility, which is a well-documented feature in practice (e.g., Bandi and Russell, [2008](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#bib.bib17); Kalnina and Linton, [2008](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#bib.bib77)). In scenario 1 – 3, the noise scales with the square root of the integrated variance $\text{IV}=\int_{0}^{1}\sigma_{s}^{2}\text{d}s$ , while in the last scenario it is a function of $\sigma_{t}$ and thus time-varying within each simulation.

The $\gamma$ parameter is the noise-to-volatility ratio of Oomen ([2006](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#bib.bib93)) which determines the relative strength of the microstructure component. We assume $\gamma=5$ , which corresponds to heavy noise pertubation (e.g., Aït-Sahalia, Jacod, and Li, [2012](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#bib.bib5); Christensen, Oomen, and Podolskij, [2014](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#bib.bib42)).

We follow standard practice in the pre-averaging literature by setting $g(x)=\min(x,1-x)$ and $k_{n}=[\theta\sqrt{n}]$ . We fix the tuning parameter at $\theta=[1,2,3]/3$ , which is in line with prior work (e.g., Aït-Sahalia, Jacod, and Li, [2012](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#bib.bib5); Christensen, Kinnebrock, and Podolskij, [2010](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#bib.bib41); Christensen, Oomen, and Podolskij, [2014](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#bib.bib42)). The threshold for jump-truncation is implemented with $\bar{\omega}=0.24$ and $c=4,5,6$ as a robustness check.696969The choice of minimal value for $c$ follows Li, Todorov, and Tauchen ([2017](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#bib.bib84)). If this parameter is chosen too small, the threshold becomes rather narrow and the truncation device starts to eliminate continuous log-returns (drawn from states of high stochastic volatility), which instills a downward bias in the truncated pre-averaged bipower variation, as opposed to the non-truncated pre-averaged realized variance. This leads to a widening of their difference, a measure of the quadratic variation of the jump component, and hence an overrejection under the null.

We compute $\Sigma_{n}^{*}$ with $L=10,15,20$ and $p=10,15,20$ following the guidelines laid out in Christensen, Podolskij, Thamrongrat, and Veliyev ([2017](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#bib.bib44)). However, the rejection rates of the jump test statistic are not sensitive to the concrete choice of tuning parameters. We therefore restrict attention to $L=10$ and $p=10$ . The corresponding tables for other combinations of tuning parameters are available on request.

Note. In the left-hand side of the table, we report the rejection rates of the feasible noise-robust pre-averaging jump test statistic in ([20](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#S2.E20)), i.e. the fraction of times (out of 10,000) $\mathcal{J}_{n}$ exceeds $\Phi^{-1}(1-\alpha)$ , where $\Phi(\cdot)$ is the standard normal distribution function and the nominal level of significance is $\alpha=0.05$ . $\theta$ controls the length of the pre-averaging window $k_{n}=[\theta\sqrt{n}]$ . $c$ is the number of local diffusive standard deviations a pre-averaged log-return must exceed to be classified as a jump and truncated to zero. $L$ is the number of subsamples in $\Sigma_{n}^{*}$ , whereas $p$ is the block length (in multiples of $k_{n}$ ). “No jump” is the null hypothesis, while $\beta$ governs the activity level of the jump process under the alternative (larger values of $\beta$ imply jumps are smaller and resemble Brownian motion closer). Each separate panel describes the structure of the microstructure noise. In the right-hand side, we report the rejection rates of the Barndorff-Nielsen and Shephard ([2006](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#bib.bib22)) jump test computed at a 5-minute sampling frequency (or $n=78$ ). The latter is not resistant to noise, so as a comparison we implement it both on the inaccessible noise-free log-price ( $p$ ) and the observable contaminated log-price ( $p^{*}$ ). Further details are available in Appendix [B](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.08962v2#A2).

Table B.2: Rejection rate of jump test statistic ( $L=10$ and $p=10$ ).
